Task 1 — Scaling Design

Your setup is sound for a class project. Key numbers: - 4 model sizes (~7M → 44M params, ~6× spread) is borderline but will show a visible trend - 5B tokens / 33 epochs gives clear signal; 3 intermediate eval points per run → 12 (N,D,loss) data points for Chinchilla, which is healthy - Budget: single A100 sweep of both conditions should cost ~$30–80 even with generous overhead — well inside $175 - Recommendation: keep A100, keep batch_size: 64. If you want to cut time in half, bump to batch_size: 128 — the blr linear scaling in the training script handles the LR adjustment automatically - Caveat: dim 128/192 use 2-layer and dim 256/512 use 3-layer, so depth is confounded with width. Worth one sentence in the write-up

Task 2 — Validation Data Format

Don't modify 4M. get_val_dataloader in pretrain_utils.py:240 always calls build_fm_pretraining_dataset regardless of use_wds. The expected structure is an ImageFolder-style layout per modality:

/project/data/val/cc12m/
  tok_rgb@224/samples/000000.npy ...
  tok_depth@224/samples/000000.npy ...
  crop_settings/samples/000000.json ...

Untar your val shards and run the reorganization script from the plan to get there.

4 read · 1 bash

There have been some changes made to the 4m_training folder. It's supposed to be the entry point to training the model on modal. The new entrypoint supposedly integrates neural data that is sharded in WDS as an input modality to train 4m, and it samples one tokenized trial for training. However, I don't see how it actually does this. It looks like the train function just calls the default ml-4m training function which doesn't support this function. Let me know what you find, and if I have misunderstood.

Now I have a complete picture. Let me explain what's actually happening.

You've misread it — the integration is there, just subtle

The key is at the very top of lib/train_4m.py, before run_train is ever called:

import fourm_neural_modalities   # line 36 — registers MEG/EEG into MODALITY_INFO + MODALITY_TRANSFORMS
patch_pretrain_utils()            # line 47 — monkey-patches 4M's live module objects

By the time run_train calls runpy.run_path("run_training_4m.py"), the fourm package already has four things replaced in-memory:

What's patched Where Effect
ud.UnifiedMasking fourm_dataloader.py:296 PresenceAwareUnifiedMasking zeros loss for absent (sentinel) neural trials
ia.PreTokenizedImageAugmenter fourm_dataloader.py:297 ThingsImageAugmenter handles optional crop_settings
ud.rename_modalities fourm_dataloader.py:299 _rename_modalities, which is where trial sampling happens
pretrain_utils.get_val_dataloader fourm_dataloader.py:310 Adds WDS support for val (solving your earlier question)

The trial sampling path specifically

The WDS pipeline calls rename_modalities on every sample. The patched version _rename_modalities fourm_dataloader.py:83-97 does the stock rename, then immediately calls _NEURAL_SPLITTER(renamed), which is a NeuralTargetSplitter. That splitter (neural_trial_transform.py:116-145):

  1. Sees the on-disk tok_meg array is (n_trials, 16, 8, 4) — multiple stacked trials per image
  2. Picks one trial index (random during train, always trial 0 during eval)
  3. Slices it into the four tok_meg_rvq* output heads — so all four RVQ layers describe the same trial

Images without neural data store a sentinel np.full((1, *shape), -1) (neural_trial_transform.py:35-42), which becomes a placeholder code, and PresenceAwareUnifiedMasking zeros the loss for that sample's neural tokens.

However: this integration does NOT reach run_scaling_experiment_2.py

This is likely the actual gap you're asking about. run_scaling_experiment_2.py launches training via subprocess:

cmd = ["torchrun", ..., "/opt/repo/ml-4m/run_training_4m.py", "--config", ...]
subprocess.run(cmd, ...)

A subprocess starts a fresh Python process. The monkey-patches from patch_pretrain_utils() live only in the current process's memory — they don't cross the subprocess boundary. So when the scaling experiment runs the treatment arm (CONDITION = "rgb_neural"), it will call the unpatched run_training_4m.py, which has no knowledge of MEG/EEG modalities or trial sampling.

To fix this for the treatment arm, run_scaling_experiment_2.py needs to invoke lib/train_4m.py train --config ... instead of calling run_training_4m.py directly:

# current (bypasses patches):
cmd = ["torchrun", "--nproc_per_node=1", "/opt/repo/ml-4m/run_training_4m.py", "--config", ...]

# needed for neural treatment arm:
cmd = ["python", "/opt/repo/4m_training/lib/train_4m.py", "train", "--config", ...]

For the control arm (rgb↔depth only, no neural data), the current subprocess call to run_training_4m.py is fine.

#42026-05-28T02:29:29.956Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/4m_training/modal_image.py in the IDE. It seems to want a specific number. if my number is higher than the total samples, what will happen? would this affect the scaling experiment somehow?

1 read · 1 bash

Now I have a complete picture. This is straightforward enough to answer directly without more searching.

How epoch_size works

epoch_size is not the number of unique samples in your dataset — it's a pacing number that controls three things:

  1. Steps per epoch: num_training_steps_per_epoch = epoch_size // (batch_size × num_tasks) — how many optimizer steps constitute one "epoch"
  2. WDS iterator length: with_epoch(epoch_size // (num_gpus × num_workers × batch_size)) — when the dataloader signals end-of-epoch to the training loop
  3. Total epochs: ceil(total_tokens × 1e9 / ((num_input_tokens + num_target_tokens) × epoch_size))

The underlying iterator (unified_datasets.py:535-546) is an infinite while True loop that auto-resets any exhausted sub-iterator. ResampledShards is also inherently infinite (samples with replacement). Neither can "run out of samples" — the epoch boundary is purely artificial, imposed by .with_epoch(N).

What happens if epoch_size > total samples

Nothing bad. The pipeline just wraps around mid-epoch. You see some images more than once within a single epoch, which is already happening (you have ~280K images at epoch_size=300,000). The training outcome — total gradient updates, tokens seen, final loss — is identical.

Effect on the scaling experiment

The only effect is eval/checkpoint granularity:

epoch_size epochs to 5B tokens eval checkpoints tokens at 1/3 / 2/3 / final
100,000 (≈ true dataset size) ~97 97 ~1.7B / 3.4B / 5B
300,000 (current) ~33 33 ~1.7B / 3.4B / 5B
1,000,000 ~10 10 ~1.7B / 3.4B / 5B

The 1/3 / 2/3 / final token values barely change because the token filter (total_tokens_seen < 1.0) always gates the 1/3 sample anyway. The scaling law fit is unaffected.

The current epoch_size: 300_000 is fine as-is. If you want epochs to correspond to one pass through all unique images, compute your true sample count and set it explicitly — but it has no effect on the training outcome or your scaling results.

I have enough context from earlier reads. Let me just verify one more thing about the warmup formula.

Both config files have the same training parameters. Here's the full evaluation:

Training Parameter Evaluation

Effective values at runtime (batch_size=64, 1 GPU, bfloat16):

Parameter Config value Computed
blr 0.0001 lr = 0.0001 × 64/256 = 2.5e-5
min_blr 0. min_lr = 0 (decays fully to zero)
warmup_tokens 0.1B warmup_steps = ⌈100M / (512 × 64)⌉ = 3,052 steps
total steps — 5B / (512 × 64) = 152,588
warmup fraction — 2.0%
num_input_tokens 256 —
num_target_tokens 256 —
loss_type mod per-modality average

Warmup assessment: 2% is fine for these model sizes (7–44M). Small models warm up fast and don't need a long ramp. No change needed.

One thing worth checking: min_blr: 0. decays LR all the way to zero. With a cosine schedule over 33 epochs that's aggressive but common. The only risk is that your 1/3-epoch checkpoint (~epoch 11) has LR at ~85% of peak, which is what you want for an intermediate loss sample — this is correct behavior.

Batch size opportunity: Your models are tiny relative to A100 VRAM. At batch_size=64 with bfloat16, the 44M model uses perhaps 500MB of VRAM — out of 40GB. Increasing to batch_size=256 or 512 would cut wall-clock time 4–8× with no change to total tokens trained. The blr scaling rule handles LR automatically (lr = blr × batch_size / 256).

Monitoring GPU Usage in Modal

Modal streams container stdout to your terminal. The simplest approach is a one-liner added to modal_image.py's ensure_fourm() or at the start of _run_train:

# add to _run_train before subprocess.run
subprocess.run(["nvidia-smi", "--query-gpu=name,memory.total,memory.used,utilization.gpu",
                "--format=csv"], check=False)

This prints GPU name, total VRAM, and current utilization at job start, so you see it in Modal logs immediately.

For live monitoring while training is running, Modal's dashboard at modal.com → your app → the running function shows real-time GPU utilization as a chart. You can also SSH into the container if you add modal.enable_memory_snapshot() or use modal shell.

For the batch size tuning loop specifically:

  1. Add this at the top of the first training step in run_training_4m.py — or just watch the Modal dashboard: python # after model.to(device), first forward pass: print(torch.cuda.memory_summary(abbreviated=True))
  2. If utilization is below 60–70%, double batch_size and adjust blr is not needed — the linear scaling is automatic.
  3. Practical target for these model sizes: try batch_size=256 first. At 44M params + bfloat16 + 512 tokens/sample, you should comfortably fit batch_size=512–1024 on an A100-40GB.

One constraint: epoch_size // (batch_size × 1) must stay > 0. At batch_size=512 and epoch_size=300,000 you get 585 steps/epoch — still fine.

1 read

finally, three more questions: 1) Do you think I might benefit from 2 simultaneous GPUs? 2) 4m says to call run_training_4m.py with torchrun, but does train_4m.py do this? 3) When neural data comes in, we have a separate validation script, such that we train on samples including neural data, but we don't do it for validation (with multiple trials for neural data, ground truth validation is difficult - but we can train by randomly sampling a trial). Would this mechanism interfere with models trained without neural data, and would this affect, and with neural data, what happens? Because the base run_training_4m.py might still attempt to run validation after epochs right, and would that attempt to include the neural data?

Now I have everything I need. All three answers flow from existing code.

Q1: Two GPUs?

Not worth it for your setup. The benefit of DDP is proportional to compute-per-gradient-sync. At 7–44M params your gradient sync is ~350KB–1.7MB — on an NVLink A100 pair that's microseconds, but the per-step compute is already tiny, so the overhead fraction is high. The scaling law also requires identical conditions across all 8 runs. If you mix 1-GPU and 2-GPU runs, the effective batch size changes, which changes LR (via the linear scaling rule), which changes what you're measuring.

The same throughput gain you'd get from a second GPU comes free from increasing batch_size. On a single A100-40GB with bfloat16 and a 44M model, you can comfortably hit batch_size=512–1024 — all of which costs nothing extra and doesn't confound your comparison. Stick with one A100, maximize batch size.

Q2: Does train_4m.py use torchrun?

No — it doesn't, and that's intentional. run_train() in lib/train_4m.py:344 manually sets the five env vars that torchrun normally injects, then calls runpy.run_path("run_training_4m.py") in the same process:

os.environ.setdefault("RANK", "0")
os.environ.setdefault("WORLD_SIZE", "1")
...
runpy.run_path(str(ml_4m_root / "run_training_4m.py"), run_name="__main__")

run_training_4m.py reads those env vars via init_distributed_mode() and sees a valid single-process DDP setup — it's happy. This is actually better than torchrun for your case because runpy.run_path runs in the same Python process, so every monkey-patch made by patch_pretrain_utils() is still live when the trainer executes. A torchrun subprocess would start a clean interpreter with no patches.

The training_env() dict already sets those same five vars, so by the time run_train() reaches setdefault, they're already present — the calls are no-ops and everything is consistent.

If you ever wanted multi-GPU, you'd need to replace runpy.run_path with a real torchrun subprocess and re-apply the patches in each spawned worker — non-trivial. Single GPU is the right call for this experiment.

Q3: Validation interference

Three cases to separate:

Control arm (rgb↔depth, no neural): No interference at all. setup_modality_info in run_training_4m.py builds modality_info from the data config's in_domains/out_domains, which are tok_rgb@224-tok_depth@224. Even though fourm_neural_modalities.register() was called at import time, neural entries are only added to MODALITY_INFO — they're not pulled into the per-run modality_info dict unless they appear in in_domains or out_domains. Standard validation runs exactly as if the patches weren't there.

Treatment arm (rgb↔depth↔neural) — in-training validation: run_training_4m.py calls evaluate() after each epoch. The patched get_val_dataloader (_neural_get_val_dataloader in fourm_dataloader.py:167) is used. Since you've set use_wds: True in your val configs, it takes the WDS branch and builds _wds_eval_loader — which sets _set_neural_target_splitter(training=False), so it always uses trial 0 deterministically rather than a random trial.

For val samples without neural data (sentinel): PresenceAwareUnifiedMasking reads the meg_mask/eeg_mask flags (neural_masking.py:86), sees they're 0, and forces those modalities' input/target budgets to zero. The loss for those tokens is never computed. This is exactly the correct behavior — absent neural data contributes 0 to the validation loss.

For your scaling experiment's result collection specifically: collect_results in run_scaling_experiment_2.py only reads tok_rgb@224_loss and tok_depth@224_loss from the log. Individual modality losses are logged separately regardless of what else is in out_domains — so even if neural validation losses are computed and logged, they don't enter the Chinchilla fit. Your scaling comparison is clean.

Your separate neural validation script: Completely orthogonal. The in-training evaluate() call will produce rgb/depth losses as normal; your script handles neural-specific eval independently. They don't share state.

One positive side effect you may not have noticed: since lib/train_4m.py is now the entrypoint for both arms and the patched get_val_dataloader supports use_wds: True, your val configs with use_wds: True will now work directly from WDS tars — no untarring needed. The plan note from Task 2 about manually reorganizing files is superseded.

it hung there for maybe two minutes, burning cycles

Two separate issues in those logs — let me address both.

Issue 1: Real sweep appeared to "do nothing"

It was actually working. Compare the two invocations:

dryrun real sweep
Command printed ... --mode sweep --test_run ... --mode sweep
What happened after quick (test exits fast → buffers flush) training started on GPU → subprocess blocked

launch_training redirects all training output to train.log:

with open(log_path, "w") as log_file:
    result = subprocess.run(cmd, env=env, stdout=log_file, stderr=subprocess.STDOUT)

And Python's stdout is block-buffered when writing to a pipe (Modal's log stream). So the print("Starting sweep:..."), print("Launching:...") etc. are sitting in Python's output buffer and never appear until the subprocess exits. In the dryrun the test run fails fast, buffers flush, you see everything. In the real sweep, training runs for hours, nothing flushes, you see nothing — and killed a training job that was actually running.

Fix: add PYTHONUNBUFFERED=1 to training_env() in modal_image.py:

This also implies I would need to change in_domains and out_domains. would this be a problem? in_domains and out_domains should match across datasets if they are the same domain right? at least, if I wanted to use the same encoder?

Good find. You don't need to change in_domains/out_domains — use modality_name_map instead. That's exactly what it's for: mapping folder names on disk to the canonical modality names the model uses internally. The @224 suffix is how 4M's registry identifies the modality (resolution included); changing in_domains would require registering a new modality name and would break the shared encoder.

The fix is two parts per config:

  1. Fix data_path to use the actual folder names (tok_rgb, tok_depth)
  2. Add modality_name_map to remap them to the canonical names

Apply to both rgb-depth-a0.5.yaml and rgb-depth-a0.5-tiny.yaml:

Done. To be clear on your question: yes, in_domains/out_domains must match across datasets that share the same encoder. tok_rgb@224 is the canonical name in 4M's MODALITY_INFO registry — it's the key the encoder looks up to get embedding dims, patch size, vocab size, etc. Renaming it to tok_rgb in the domain list would require registering a separate modality entry and would silently give the two datasets different encoder heads.

modality_name_map is the right layer to fix the mismatch: it's applied at the WDS decode step, before the sample ever reaches the model, so folder names on disk can differ from the canonical modality names.

One follow-up to check: does your cc12m data also use tok_rgb (no @224) folder names, or does it actually have tok_rgb@224 folders? The cc12m data_path in the config still uses tok_rgb@224. If cc12m folders are also without @224, that config needs the same treatment.

The 0-byte issue has two causes working together: launch_training redirects the subprocess to a file but Modal's volume file listing can be stale, AND if the job is still running, modal volume get is the only way to pull a snapshot.

But the better fix is to tee the subprocess output to Modal's stdout so you see it live in the dashboard, while still writing to the log file for post-hoc inspection. Change launch_training in run_scaling_experiment_2.py:

Now every line from lib/train_4m.py and run_training_4m.py streams live to Modal's dashboard, and the log file gets the same content flushed line-by-line (so it won't be 0 bytes).

For the current stuck run, you can pull a snapshot of the log file right now with:

modal volume get project /data/scaling_sweep/dim128_layer2_tok5.0B/test_run/train.log ./train.log

(adjust the path if needed). That will tell you whether the subprocess is crashing during import or actually running.

#132026-05-28T03:20:45.332Z

to be clear, this will simultaneously right to train.log and also to stdout? so if it crashes or I quit, I can still view train.log?

Yes, exactly. The Popen loop writes each line to both log_file and sys.stdout before moving to the next line, and log_file.flush() forces the write to disk immediately. So:

  • While running: output streams live to Modal's dashboard
  • If it crashes: everything up to the crash is already in train.log on the volume
  • If you kill the Modal job: same — whatever was flushed before the kill is preserved

The only content you'd lose is any output that was buffered inside the subprocess itself at the moment of a hard kill. Since PYTHONUNBUFFERED=1 is in the environment, Python's own stdout buffer is disabled, so that window is essentially zero.

what might cause this error now?

You're right — let me think about this differently. The stock 4M val loader always uses MultiModalDatasetFolder (ImageFolder-style) and silently ignores use_wds for val. Our patch broke that by actually respecting use_wds: True and routing to WDS, which then tries to open the bare directory as a tar file.

The simplest fix is: change the val configs back to use_wds: False. The val data at /project/data/val/cc12m is in ImageFolder format (per-modality subdirectories), not WDS tars. The stock loader handles that correctly.

#202026-05-28T03:47:01.734Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/cfgs/neural/4m/modal/data/rgb-depth-a0.5.yaml in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. what does weights: [1.0, 1.0] do exactly, for sampling weights?

MixtureDataset normalizes the weights into a probability distribution and uses np.random.choice to pick which dataset to draw from at each step. So [1.0, 1.0] → [0.5, 0.5] — every sample has a 50/50 chance of coming from cc12m vs things, regardless of the actual sizes of the two datasets.

Concretely with your data: - cc12m has 53 shards, things has 27 shards - With equal weights, roughly half your training tokens come from things even though it's the smaller dataset

If you wanted to weight proportionally to dataset size you'd use [53, 27] (or [2.0, 1.0] approximately). Equal weights as you have them is a deliberate choice to oversample things — which makes sense for the neural condition since that's where your neural data lives. For the control rgb_only arm it doesn't matter much either way since both datasets are just RGB+depth.

#212026-05-28T03:48:26.613Z

I would be disproportionately sampling from the same images in things though, compared to CC12M though no? there are 25k samples in things, and there are 250k samples in cc12m

Yes, exactly right. With [1.0, 1.0] and your 10:1 size ratio, over a full 5B token run things images get seen ~196 times vs ~19 times for cc12m. That's heavy memorization territory for a 25k-image dataset.

For proportional sampling use [10.0, 1.0]:

weights: [10.0, 1.0]

This gives cc12m ~90.9% of draws and things ~9.1%, so each image from both datasets gets seen roughly the same number of times per token of training.

One nuance for the neural treatment arm: you might actually want to slightly oversample things beyond proportional (e.g. [5.0, 1.0]) to ensure the model sees enough neural-paired samples, since only ~10% of things images have neural data. But for the control arm (rgb_only), strict proportional [10.0, 1.0] is the right call — there's no reason to oversample things there.

Apply the change to both rgb-depth-a0.5.yaml and rgb-depth-a0.5-tiny.yaml.

#222026-05-28T03:52:30.450Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/cfgs/neural/4m/modal/data/rgb-depth-a0.5.yaml in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. the gpu used 28 gib at it's peak - think I can increase batch size more?

With 28 GiB used and a 40 GiB A100, you have ~12 GiB headroom. For dim=128 alone you could push to batch_size=640–768 safely.

However — before increasing, the more important question is whether dim=512 (the largest model in your sweep) fits at batch_size=512 at all. Transformer activation memory scales with d_model, so dim=512 with 3+3 layers uses roughly 4–6x more activation memory than dim=128 with 2+2 layers. It's plausible dim=512 at batch_size=512 would OOM on a 40 GB card.

The practical approach: 1. Uncomment dim=512 in MODEL_CONFIGS and run it first at the current batch_size=512 to see peak memory 2. If it fits with headroom, then increase batch_size for all configs 3. If dim=512 already pushes close to 40 GiB at batch_size=512, you'll need to decrease batch_size for the larger models

One option the sweep already supports is using different configs per model — you could have batch_size: 512 in the 2-layer config and batch_size: 128 or batch_size: 256 in the 3-layer config. The LR auto-adjusts via blr * batch_size / 256 so you don't need to touch blr.

The LR auto-adjusts: at batch_size=256, lr = blr * 256/256 = 0.0001 exactly (back to base LR). The 2-layer models keep batch_size: 512 → lr = 0.0002, which is fine.

One note: with batch_size=256 instead of 512 for the 3-layer models, each step processes half the tokens, so they'll take roughly 2× as many steps to reach 5B tokens. Wall time for dim=512 doubles, but it will fit in your budget.

3 edit · 1 read

Yes, comfortably. The memory scales roughly linearly with d_model. Going from dim=512 to dim=256 halves activation memory, so at batch=640 (1.25× the current 512) you'd expect:

0.5 × 1.25 × 38.32 GiB ≈ 24 GiB peak

That leaves ~15 GiB of headroom on a 40 GiB card.

Since both dim=256 and dim=512 use the same 3-layer base config, the cleanest fix is to override batch_size per-dim inside generate_configs rather than baking a single value into the YAML:

#252026-05-28T04:09:01.248Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/run_scaling_experiment_2.py in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. what is Eval vs Fixed eval during training?

Regular Eval: uses the same stochastic Dirichlet masking as training — each sample gets a random number of input/target tokens drawn from the alpha distribution. Loss varies slightly between runs over the same val set because the masking differs each time.

Fixed Eval: uses a hard-coded, deterministic token budget — typically 128 input tokens and 128 target tokens per sample, every time. The exact same masking is applied across all checkpoints and all model sizes, so losses are directly comparable.

For your Chinchilla fit, Fixed Eval is the right one to use — and collect_results already does this, filtering for "[Fixed Eval (cc12m)] tok_rgb@224_loss" keys. Regular Eval numbers would add noise to your (N, D, loss) triples because two checkpoints at the same training budget could have slightly different losses just from masking variance.

The relevant config line is:

fixed_eval: True

That's already set in both your scaling model configs, so you're good.

1 bash

Using the plan's parameter estimates and your dim=128 measurement as the anchor — wall time scales as N × D (FLOPs), and D is fixed at 5B tokens for all models:

Model Params Time Cost
dim=128, 2e2d 7.5M 90 min $3.00
dim=192, 2e2d 11M 132 min $4.40
dim=256, 3e3d 17M 204 min $6.80
dim=512, 3e3d 44M 528 min $17.60
Total ~16 hrs ~$32

One sweep = ~$32. Two conditions (control + neural) = ~$64, well inside your $175 budget with ~$110 left for reruns and debugging.

The dim=512 model dominates — it costs more than the other three combined because its 6× parameter advantage over dim=128 translates directly to 6× the compute at fixed token budget. If you need to cut cost, dropping dim=512 saves ~$18 per condition but weakens the Chinchilla fit (smaller N range).

1 bash · 1 read

In the log.txt, it reports Fixed eval stats. It just reports a single value for each modality type there, but how exactly is it calculating it? There might be mixed tasks with different masking percents right, how does it deal with that? Also, Is it doing fixed seeding? For reference, run_training_4m.py is where it happens. We also implemented task-specified validation, where a yaml file specifies the types of tasks to do in-loop validation on. Will this operate in the same way as the stock 4m in-loop validation?

Here's what's actually happening in each part:

How the loss is averaged

evaluate() iterates the entire fixed-eval dataloader and accumulates a MetricLogger that computes global_avg per modality. Each sample still goes through PresenceAwareUnifiedMasking with input_tokens_range=(128,128) and target_tokens_range=(128,128) — so the total token budget is locked, but the Dirichlet alphas still randomly distribute those 128 target tokens across modalities per sample. The logged tok_rgb@224_loss is the mean cross-entropy over all samples where rgb appeared as a target, averaged across the whole val set. With a large enough val set the variance from random masking washes out, which is why it's stable enough to use for Chinchilla.

Seeding

Not re-seeded before eval. The seed is set once at startup (seed=0 default, line 395), then the RNG state evolves through all training steps. By epoch K, the masking randomness in eval reflects whatever RNG state training left behind — it's not deterministic between checkpoints. This is fine for your use case since you're averaging over the full val set anyway.

Task-specified validation

I'd need to see that code to say for sure. The key question is whether it calls evaluate() in run_training_4m.py (same path, same averaging, same PresenceAwareUnifiedMasking in effect) or does its own thing. Where is that implementation — is it in lib/ or somewhere in ml-4m?

#282026-05-28T06:46:05.194Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/4m_training/lib/in_loop_val.py in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. it would be in lib/in_loop_val.py and related files, and monkey patched in, I think..

2 read · 2 bash

Now I have the full picture. Three things to know:

1. How the loss is calculated

Both paths use the same underlying logic: call model(mod_dict, num_encoder_tokens=n_in, num_decoder_tokens=n_out) and get back a per-modality loss dict. The fixed token counts eliminate range variance, but Dirichlet still randomly distributes those 128 tokens across modalities per sample. The reported value is an average over all samples. There is no re-seeding before each eval — the RNG state is whatever training left it at.

2. In-loop val is NOT the same as stock fixed eval in two important ways

Stock fixed eval ([Fixed Eval (cc12m)]): - Iterates the entire val set, weighted average via MetricLogger.global_avg - Stable enough for Chinchilla fitting

Your in-loop val ([in-loop val: {name}]): - Averages over only n_batches batches (default 4 in build_suite_fn), simple sum/count - With batch_size=512 × 4 batches = 2048 samples — much noisier - Errors are swallowed silently (won't crash training but you can miss failures) - Stats go under a different prefix entirely

3. For your scaling experiment this is fine

collect_results parses [Fixed Eval (cc12m)] tok_rgb@224_loss — that's the stock path, full val set, stable. The in-loop val runs additionally for your own diagnostic purposes but isn't what feeds Chinchilla. Just make sure your val YAML config data path is correct so the stock fixed eval actually runs; if data_loaders_fixed_eval is empty, log.txt will have no [Fixed Eval] lines and collect_results will return nothing.

#292026-05-28T06:47:57.592Z

so will the stock fixed eval still run? how does the in-loop val get run?

3 read · 2 bash

Both questions have clear answers now.

Stock fixed eval: yes, always runs. run_train imports the trainer as a module via _load_trainer_module, then calls _drive_trainer_main → trainer.main(args). Inside main, the normal data_loaders_fixed_eval loop runs every eval_freq epochs using the WDS val loaders built by your patched _neural_get_val_dataloader. Those produce [Fixed Eval (cc12m)] lines in log.txt — exactly what collect_results parses.

In-loop val: opt-in, off by default. In _drive_trainer_main (lines 407–415):

if getattr(args, "in_loop_val_tasks", None):
    install_in_loop_validation(trainer, build_suite_fn(args), ...)

It only activates if in_loop_val_tasks is set in your model config YAML. Neither 4m-neural-2e-2d-scaling.yaml nor 4m-neural-3e-3d-scaling.yaml has that field, so in-loop val is not running during your scaling sweep. The two eval systems are completely independent — stock fixed eval is always on, in-loop val is an opt-in extra.

#302026-05-28T06:50:22.917Z

if I include neural data in the validation set, it might break the stock? the neural data has multiple trials per sample, and the special neural validation I think handles this through some trial sampling strategy.

The concern is valid for the stock ImageFolder path, but not for your setup. _wds_eval_loader already installs NeuralTargetSplitter(training=False) before building the pipeline:

def _wds_eval_loader(...):
    fourm_neural_modalities.register(training=False)
    _set_neural_target_splitter(training=False)  # ← deterministic trial 0

So the trial-sampling mechanism that "special neural validation" uses is already wired into every WDS val loader — including the one called by the stock fixed eval. Trial 0 is always picked, and PresenceAwareUnifiedMasking zeros the budget for samples without neural data (the 90% of THINGS without paired recordings).

For your scaling sweep specifically: - Control arm (rgb_only): val path only has [tok_rgb,tok_depth] — no neural data involved, no issue - Neural arm (rgb_neural): if you add neural modalities to the val path and in_domains/out_domains, the WDS loader handles it correctly through the same patch stack

The only case that would break is if neural val data somehow went through _ORIG_GET_VAL (the ImageFolder path), which can't handle stacked trial tensors. But that path is only taken when use_wds: False — since your val configs all have use_wds: True, you'll never hit it.

Bottom line: you can safely include neural data in the val set for the neural arm. The rgb/depth losses in log.txt remain valid for Chinchilla comparison against the control arm.

#342026-05-28T07:56:30.314Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/run_scaling_experiment_2.py in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. you suggested a bunch of alpha tasks, which do make sense. but couldn't you combine them together? would there be any detriments?

Yes, you can combine them — and it's actually the standard 4M approach. A Dirichlet mixture config is already a weighted combination of multiple masking strategies in a single YAML. Option B is essentially that combination already: pixel→neural + neural→pixel + all2all in one config.

The only reason to keep Options A and C separate would be an ablation study (does training with MEG-only-prediction hurt visual reconstruction? does all2all underperform bidirectional?). For a scaling law study you don't need that — you just need a single training objective per arm.

Potential detriment of combining: each component gets proportionally less training time. With 3 components at [0.4, 0.4, 0.2], the pixel→neural task sees 40% of THINGS batches instead of 100%. But since you want both directions, this is a feature not a bug — the model learns to go both ways.

Practical recommendation: just use Option B as the single alpha config for the treatment arm. It already bakes in all three strategies. The only reason to consider Options A or C would be if you later want to run a targeted ablation, in which case you'd have the separate files ready to swap in.

Should I proceed with just the one bidirectional config per modality (6 files → 2 files: rgb-meg-bidirectional-all2all.yaml and rgb-eeg-bidirectional-all2all.yaml), or do you still want all three options available?

#362026-05-28T08:10:45.380Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/cfgs/neural/4m/modal/data/rgb-depth-meg-a0.5.yaml in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. any suggestions on sampling weights between cc12m and things when neural data is involved?

The core tradeoff: THINGS weight determines how often neural heads get gradient, but CC12M weight determines how well visual reconstruction generalizes (which is what Chinchilla measures).

At [9, 1] (control arm default): THINGS gets ~10% of batches. With bidirectional alphas (40% of THINGS batches target neural), only ~4% of all training steps actually predict MEG/EEG. The neural heads learn very little.

At [5, 1] (what you just set): THINGS gets ~17% of batches → ~7% of all steps predict neural. This is roughly 2× the neural signal. Reasonable choice.

At [3, 1]: ~25% THINGS → ~10% neural prediction steps. Stronger neural learning but each of the 25K THINGS images is seen ~66× over training (vs ~33× in the control arm), which increases visual overfitting risk on that small set.

The key constraint is the CC12M Fixed Eval: that's what collect_results reads for the Chinchilla fit. If over-sampling THINGS degrades CC12M val loss (because the model spends too much time on 25K images instead of learning general visual features), the scaling curves for both arms drift apart for the wrong reason.

My suggestion: [5, 1] is defensible — it doubles neural exposure without extreme THINGS repetition. I'd also apply the same [5, 1] to the EEG config for consistency. One thing to watch on the first training run: if THINGS val loss (neural modalities) is still very high at the end, you may want to push to [3, 1]. If CC12M val loss is notably worse than the control arm, pull back to [7, 1].

Do you want me to update the EEG config to match [5, 1] as well?

1 edit

Lastly, on my first run, 2e-2d with 7m params, modal stats is showing GPU occasionally spiking up to 38 GB. Please provide reasonable batch size estimates for the other models, especially considering there may be multiple other modalities (including 4 meg modalities). do you think 4 meg modalities be difficult to train, how will that affect my costs?

Good question. Let me work through both memory and cost carefully.

Why MEG adds almost no memory overhead

The transformer always receives exactly num_input_tokens + num_target_tokens = 512 tokens, regardless of how many modalities exist. MEG tokens (4 × 128 = 512 tokens) get masked and sampled down to that budget before the GPU forward pass. The extra GPU memory from MEG is only the embedding tables (4 × 512 × dim) and the decoder logit tensors for THINGS batches (~56 MB peak). At dim=512 that's about 4 MB of weights and ~56 MB of peak logits — completely negligible. Use the same batch sizes for the neural arm as the control arm.

The 38 GB number is PyTorch's memory reservation, not its working set

PyTorch's CUDA caching allocator grabs a large pool upfront so it doesn't need to request memory from CUDA on every allocation. The actual working peak for a 7M model at bs=640 is probably 16–20 GB; PyTorch just reserves the rest. The 38 GB spike is normal and doesn't mean you're close to a real OOM.

Batch size analysis

Memory scales roughly as: M ≈ bs × (C_fixed + C_act × dim × layers) where C_fixed is dominated by decoder logit tensors (vocab-size dependent, not dim-dependent) and C_act is transformer activation memory.

Fitting this model to your two data points (dim=128 2e2d bs=640 → 38 GB reserved; dim=512 3e3d bs=512 → OOM):

Model Dim Arch Current bs Recommended bs Notes
7M 128 2e2d 640 640 fine, keep
11M 192 2e2d 640 512 no override in generate_configs → likely OOM
17M 256 3e3d 640 512 current override of 640 is borderline
44M 512 3e3d 256 256 confirmed safe

The dim=192 case is the silent bug: generate_configs only overrides batch_size for layers == 3, so dim=192 2e2d inherits bs=640 from the base YAML. That likely OOMs. I'll fix the override logic to cover all sizes:

Cost impact

Total GPU compute = total_tokens × compute_per_token, independent of batch size. Halving batch size just doubles the number of steps with the same total GPU-hours. The only side effect is the effective LR scales with batch size (lr = blr × bs / 256), so bs=512 trains with a slightly lower LR than bs=640 — acceptable for a Chinchilla experiment where you care about loss vs tokens, not loss vs steps.

Will 4 MEG modalities be difficult?

Computationally: no, same cost as control. But MEG prediction is inherently noisy — brain responses to the same image vary across trials, subjects, and frequency bands. The model will converge to higher residual loss for MEG tokens than for depth tokens. That's not a failure; it reflects the signal-to-noise ratio of MEG data. The scaling experiment should still show a clear trend (larger models predict MEG better than smaller ones), which is exactly the hypothesis you're testing.

Cost for three conditions: 3× the control arm — roughly ~$96 total at $3.50/hr A100, well within your $175 budget.

The problem is clear. fourm_neural_modalities.py has three stale names from an older asymmetric design that no longer exist: - MEG_OUT_SOURCE_PATH — imported but never used in the file body; doesn't exist in neural_constants.py - MEG_RVQ_OUT_MODALITIES — used on line 156, should be MEG_RVQ_MODALITIES - EEG_OUT_MODALITY — used on line 158, should be EEG_MODALITY

The current symmetric design merged input and output modalities, but lines 153–158 weren't updated. Fix:

On your model size question — yes, MEG increases model size, and the amount is non-trivial for small models.

Each tok_meg_rvq0..3 is a fully independent modality with its own encoder embedding + decoder embedding. Looking at the _NEURAL_MODALITY_INFO construction in that file:

Per RVQ layer:  MegRVQEncoderEmbedding (512×dim embedding + 128×dim positional)
              + MegRVQDecoderEmbedding (512×dim embedding + dim×512 projection + 128×dim positional)
              ≈ 1400 × dim params per layer

4 MEG layers:  ≈ 5,600 × dim params total
EEG:          ≈ 16,500 × dim params (larger because vocab=8192 vs 512)
Model Baseline + MEG + EEG
7M (dim=128) 7M +0.7M (10%) +2.1M (30%)
11M (dim=192) 11M +1.1M (10%) +3.2M (29%)
17M (dim=256) 17M +1.4M (8%) +4.2M (25%)
44M (dim=512) 44M +2.9M (7%) +8.4M (19%)

The transformer backbone is unchanged — these are all embedding table weights at the edges. The transformer still processes exactly 512 tokens. So training compute (FLOPs) is identical across conditions; only the parameter count changes.

EEG actually adds more params than MEG despite being simpler, purely because its codebook vocab is 8192 vs MEG's 512. This means if you're comparing scaling laws between conditions, be precise about which "N" you're using — the transformer N or the total model N (including embeddings).

#402026-05-28T08:47:40.041Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/run_scaling_experiment_2.py in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. should scaling law evaluation not include embedding dimensions?

4 read · 3 edit

Yes, and it matters a lot for your models. At dim=128, over half the 7M parameters are embedding lookup tables (RGB vocab=16384 and depth vocab=8192 dominate). Including them inflates N for small models and skews the scaling exponents — exactly the opposite of what Chinchilla's N is supposed to measure.

The standard practice (Kaplan, Hoffmann et al.) uses transformer-backbone-only N because: 1. Embeddings are lookup tables — O(1) compute per token, not O(dim²) like attention/FFN 2. Your conditions have different embedding sizes (control vs MEG vs EEG), making total-N comparisons misleading across arms 3. The interesting scaling signal is in the backbone, not the vocab tables

Transformer-only N for your models:

Each encoder block: 4×dim² (attn) + 8×dim² (FFN, gelu 4×) = 12×dim²
Each decoder block: 4×dim² (cross-attn) + 4×dim² (self-attn) + 8×dim² (FFN) = 16×dim²

Formula: (12+16) × layers × dim² = 28 × layers × dim²

Model Total N (logged) Transformer N Spread
dim=128 2e2d ~7M ~0.9M
dim=192 2e2d ~11M ~2.1M
dim=256 3e3d ~17M ~5.5M
dim=512 3e3d ~44M ~22M
Range 6× 24× much better for fitting

I'll replace the log-based extract_model_size call in collect_results with an analytic computation:

Done. collect_results now records both: - "N" — transformer backbone only (28 × layers × dim²), used by fit_scaling_law for Chinchilla - "N_total" — total params from train.log (includes all embedding tables)

On the comparability question: tok_depth@224 loss from [Fixed Eval (cc12m)] is comparable across all three conditions. The CC12M val set and evaluation protocol are identical — same fixed 128 in / 128 out token budget, same alpha masking. The only differences are intended experimental effects: - The neural arm trains on ~6% fewer depth examples from THINGS (component 0 uses depth as input only, not target), which should slightly degrade depth reconstruction - The shared backbone capacity is split across neural decoding heads in the neural arms

Those effects are exactly what you're measuring — any difference in the tok_depth@224 scaling curve between rgb_only and pixel_meg/pixel_eeg reflects the cost/benefit of adding neural modalities to the backbone.

#412026-05-28T09:16:51.110Z

The user selected the lines 58 to 58 from /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/4m_training/modal_train.py: test_run

This may or may not be related to the current task. one thing I'm immediately noticing with the MEG included models is that the tok_depth fixed cc12m evals are just not as good at the smallest model. is this reaosnable? do we expect this to be the case across all model sizes?

Yes, this is expected, and the effect should be most pronounced at the smallest model and shrink with scale. Here's why:

Why the neural arm hurts depth at small models:

The backbone (dim=128, 2 layers) has a very limited representation capacity. During the ~17% of batches that contain THINGS samples, the bidirectional alpha config forces the backbone to: - Encode MEG tokens alongside visual tokens in the same 2-layer encoder - Produce both visual reconstructions and MEG predictions through a single 2-layer decoder

These competing objectives consume a disproportionate fraction of the available capacity at dim=128. The gradient signal from MEG prediction (especially neural→pixel batches) points in a direction orthogonal to pure visual reconstruction, and a shallow backbone can't compartmentalize them. The CC12M eval surfaces this because the visual representations are slightly degraded.

Why it should shrink with model size:

Each doubling of dim roughly quadruples backbone capacity (O(dim²) for attention and FFN), while MEG adds a fixed embedding overhead that doesn't scale with the backbone. By dim=512 (3 layers, ~64× the 128 backbone capacity), the model has enough room to develop representations that handle both visual quality and neural prediction without one crowding out the other. The THINGS samples may even help at large scale — paired MEG/image data can act as a structured regularizer that improves visual feature quality.

What to watch for:

If the depth loss gap between rgb_only and pixel_meg: - Grows with model size → neural modalities are actively harmful at all scales (unexpected) - Shrinks with model size → capacity bottleneck is the explanation (expected)
- Crosses at some N → neural arm is better at large scale (best case, scientifically interesting)

The crossover scenario would be the most interesting finding for a class project — it gives you an empirically grounded "break-even" model size for multimodal neural integration. Keep an eye on the 256 and 512 dim results; if the gap is visibly smaller there, the scaling law fit will naturally capture this as a difference in the fitted alpha exponent between conditions.

#422026-05-28T09:29:51.348Z

my rgb -> depth had a strong depth from rgb mixture of alphas, which is probably the best modality. perhaps this is why? how should I approach this?

Good catch — this is a real confounder. Let me walk through what's happening and what to do.

What the control arm's alpha config gives depth:

The mix_mod7_all2all_rgb2all_a0.5.yaml used by the control arm has an rgb2all component where RGB has input_alpha ≈ 1000 (always in) and depth has target_alpha high (always target). That's direct, strong RGB→depth supervision. Your MEG alpha config has no equivalent — Component 0 puts depth as input to predict MEG, not as a target; Component 1 predicts depth from MEG context, not RGB; Component 2 uses alpha=0.5 which is only a moderate depth-target signal.

How to tell whether it's the alpha config or backbone capacity:

Check [Fixed Eval (cc12m)] tok_rgb@224_loss for the same small model across conditions. The CC12M section is identical in both data configs — same domains, same alphas. So:

  • RGB loss also worse in MEG arm → genuine backbone capacity competition from MEG tokens
  • RGB loss roughly equal but depth worse → it's the alpha config asymmetry, not capacity

How to fix it if you want a clean comparison:

Add a 4th component to the MEG/EEG alpha configs that preserves a pixel→depth training signal. The simplest version mirrors the control arm by adding a component where visual tokens are strong targets while MEG is just available as context:

sampling_weights: [0.25, 0.25, 0.25, 0.25]
alphas_mixture:
  tok_rgb@224:
    input_alphas: [1000., 0.,    0.5,  1000.]   # comp 3: RGB in
    target_alphas: [0.,  0.5,   0.5,   0.5]
  tok_depth@224:
    input_alphas: [1000., 0.,    0.5,  0.5]
    target_alphas: [0.,   0.5,   0.5,  1000.]   # comp 3: depth strongly out
  tok_meg_rvqN:
    input_alphas: [0.,   1000.,  0.5,  0.5]     # MEG available as context
    target_alphas: [0.5,  0.,    0.5,  0.]      # but not targeted in comp 3

This makes Component 3 a visual-only reconstruction component identical in spirit to the control arm's rgb2all component, while preserving the three neural components.

Practical recommendation:

Before re-running, check RGB loss first. If RGB is comparable, the depth gap is almost entirely the alpha asymmetry — the fix is straightforward and worth making for a clean comparison. If RGB is also worse, the story is more interesting (capacity competition is real), and the alpha fix still helps isolate the two effects.

The data tells a clear story. Let me break it down:

RGB is also worse in the MEG arm (CC12M eval):

Model Δ depth Δ RGB
192 dim +0.361 +0.204
512 dim +0.295 +0.154

Both gaps are shrinking with scale — this confirms the depth gap is not primarily the alpha config asymmetry. It's genuine backbone capacity competition. The MEG arm is learning slightly worse visual representations across the board because the shared backbone also handles MEG reconstruction.

The THINGS visual losses are confounded (don't use them as your metric).

In the MEG arm, the THINGS alpha config has depth as an input in Component 0 and as an output from MEG context in Component 1. The pixel-only arm has a direct RGB→depth supervision signal in its THINGS alpha. That's why pixel-only shows much better THINGS depth (7.32 vs 8.39) — different training objectives on that 17% of batches, not model quality. Stick to CC12M fixed eval for fair cross-condition comparison.

MEG prediction is actually working: - rvq0 loss ≈ 3.2 vs chance ≈ 6.24 nats (ln(512)) — genuinely learned - rvq1-3 ≈ 5.8-6.2 — barely above chance, fine detail is hard to predict from images

This is scientifically sensible: high-level MEG structure (rvq0) is predictable from visual context; fine detail (rvq3) is mostly unpredictable. That's the real signal.

What to do:

Don't add the RGB→depth component right now. The shrinking gap across scale is the finding — adding the component changes the training objective and muddies the story. The clean message is: at small N, adding a neural modality costs ~0.2-0.36 visual loss units; at larger N that cost shrinks.

Wait for the 256 dim results. You need all four model sizes to fit the scaling curve. With two points you can't tell whether the gap is closing linearly, faster, or hitting a floor.

The actual hypothesis to test: Does the visual loss gap (loss_meg - loss_rgb_only) scale as a power law in N? If it decays as N^{-γ}, you can estimate the crossover point where adding MEG becomes cost-neutral. That's the interesting class-project result.

More data won't help — the gap is architectural, not data-limited. Model scaling is already doing the right thing.

256 dim 3e3d ~5.5M +0.108 +0.089
512 dim 3e3d ~22M +0.295 +0.154

The 256 model has the smallest gap. The 512 model is actually worse than 256 for the MEG arm despite being larger — 7.851 → 8.020 depth is a regression, not improvement. For pixel-only, 512 is correctly better than 256 (7.743 → 7.725). So the non-monotonicity is specific to the MEG condition, not noise.

What might explain this:

The most likely cause is that 512 dim (bs=256) hasn't fully converged. The linear batch-size scaling rule (lr = blr × bs/256) means 512 trains with half the effective learning rate of 192/256. In practice, for a fixed token budget the smaller-batch run takes more gradient steps but each step is noisier and at a lower lr — the loss curve can plateau earlier or later than expected. If the 512 MEG loss curve is still visibly declining at the final checkpoint, that's the answer.

Practical check: Look at the intermediate eval rows in checkpoints/log.txt for the 512 MEG run. Is the depth loss still dropping at the last eval, or has it plateaued? Compare the slope to the 256 MEG run.

Impact on the Chinchilla fit:

A non-monotonic N vs loss relationship breaks the power-law assumption. The fit will still run (it's just BFGS on a smooth surface) but the fitted exponents won't mean much physically if the 512 point is an outlier. Your best options:

  • If 512 MEG is under-converged: increase total_tokens to 10B for 512 only and re-run
  • If converged: the story shifts from "scaling closes the gap" to "the gap has non-trivial structure" — possibly the 256 sweet spot is where layer depth (3) first provides enough routing capacity, and beyond that width creates stronger cross-modal attention competition. That's still a valid finding, just a different one.

The pixel-only scaling is clean (7.852 → 7.743 → 7.725) and will give a good Chinchilla fit for the control arm. Focus on getting the MEG arm's 512 model right before fitting.

The log reveals the problem clearly. It's not a convergence issue — it's gradient clipping.

The model is converged. LR at epoch 32 is 7.87e-8 (essentially zero, cosine schedule finished). CC12M depth at the last two evals: 8.026 → 8.020. Flat.

But gradient norms grew monotonically and exceeded clip_grad: 3.0 for most of training:

Epoch Grad norm LR Clipped?
0 1.36 6.7e-5 No
9 1.92 8.3e-5 No
12 3.55 7.0e-5 Yes (~84%)
16 5.15 5.2e-5 Yes (~58%)
22 6.55 2.4e-5 Yes (~46%)
28 6.78 4.7e-6 Yes (~44%)
32 5.98 7.9e-8 Yes (~50%)

From epoch 12 through 32 — roughly two-thirds of total training — every gradient step was clipped to 44–84% of its actual magnitude. The 256 dim model at bs=512 computes gradients over 2× more samples per step, which reduces gradient variance and keeps norms lower. The comparison is unfair.

Yes, rerun on 80GB A100 with bs=512. Also raise clip_grad from 3.0 to 6.0 at the same time, because even at bs=512 the norms might reach 4–5 (they won't drop by √2 exactly — the true gradient component doesn't reduce with batch size, only the noise component does):

The problem is clear: gradient clipping was active for the entire second half of training. From epoch 12 onwards, every step was processed at 44–75% of its intended magnitude because clip_grad: 3.0 was set for a bs=256 model whose gradient variance is twice that of the bs=512 models.

Yes, rerun on 80GB with bs=512. I'll add a --large_gpu flag to enable it cleanly:

To rerun only the 512 dim model, set MODEL_CONFIGS to just that entry and launch:

modal run 4m_training/modal_train.py --condition pixel_meg --large_gpu

What this fixes and what to watch for:

The gradient norm spike from ~1.5 → 6.8 over training is real — it's not random noise, it's the optimizer finding a curvature-heavy region during cosine decay. At bs=512 those norms will likely land in the 3–5 range (variance ∝ 1/√bs), so you may still see occasional clipping. Check the new run's log around epoch 12–15: if grad norms stay under 4, the fix worked. If they're still 5+, consider bumping clip_grad from 3.0 to 6.0 specifically in the 3e3d configs.

Keep clip_grad: 3.0 unchanged for the first rerun so the only difference vs the 256 dim run is GPU memory / batch size. That gives you a clean comparison.

#462026-05-28T19:17:25.162Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/4m_training/modal_train.py in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. should I continue training from the last 512 checkpoint? will it keep token counts correct? or should I just restart and keep it at the fixed 5B? should I have gradient clipping at all?

2 read · 1 edit

Don't continue — restart from scratch.

Three reasons:

  1. The schedule is spent. The cosine LR hit 7.87e-8 at epoch 32, which is functionally zero. Continuing from this checkpoint would require restarting the LR schedule from a non-zero value, creating a step discontinuity in the learning rate. The model would effectively start a second training arc from an already-converged state — unusual behavior that produces strange intermediate checkpoints.

  2. Token counts would be misaligned for Chinchilla. collect_results reads total_tokens_seen_b from each intermediate eval. If you continue, the first eval checkpoint appears at ~6.7B tokens, the next at ~8.3B, final at ~10B. Your other models have data points at 1.7B / 3.4B / 5B. With no overlap in D values, the joint L(N,D) fit will be poorly constrained — you can't disentangle N and D effects if the large model only has data at much higher D than the small models.

  3. Auto-resume will pick up the old checkpoint. The training code has auto_resume: true by default. If the output directory already exists with a checkpoint, any new launch will resume from there rather than starting fresh. Either delete the dim512_layer3_tok5.0B/checkpoints/ directory first, or rename it to dim512_layer3_tok5.0B_bs256_bad/ to preserve the old data while getting a clean directory for the rerun.

On gradient clipping:

Keep it, but raise the threshold. clip_grad=3.0 is appropriate when gradients rarely exceed it — it's a safety net for bad batches and early instability. Here it was acting as a systematic training constraint for 20+ epochs. Two things are happening simultaneously: (1) 512 dim has more parameters and intrinsically higher gradient norm (scales as √N), and (2) bs=256 has higher per-step variance than bs=512.

Set clip_grad: 6.0 for the 512 dim configs specifically. The 192/256 models almost certainly never hit 6.0, so it won't affect them — but document the change.

Critical: also rerun 512 pixel_only with the same new settings (bs=512, clip_grad=6.0 on 80GB). The pixel_only 512 ran at bs=256 too, so its visual losses are also slightly underestimated. If you fix MEG but not pixel_only, the cross-condition comparison at 512 dim is still unfair.

Concretely, update the 3e3d configs before the rerun:

Summary of what to do:

  1. Rename the bad 512 dim run directories (both conditions) so auto-resume doesn't pick them up: bash mv /project/data/scaling_sweep/pixel_meg/dim512_layer3_tok5.0B \ /project/data/scaling_sweep/pixel_meg/dim512_layer3_tok5.0B_bs256_bad mv /project/data/scaling_sweep/rgb_only/dim512_layer3_tok5.0B \ /project/data/scaling_sweep/rgb_only/dim512_layer3_tok5.0B_bs256_bad

  2. Set MODEL_CONFIGS to only the 512 entry, run both conditions back-to-back: bash modal run 4m_training/modal_train.py --condition rgb_only --large_gpu modal run 4m_training/modal_train.py --condition pixel_meg --large_gpu

The --large_gpu flag sets bs=512 and clip_grad=6.0 for dim=512 only. All other dims are unaffected.

#472026-05-28T21:22:01.674Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/run_scaling_experiment_2.py in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. I'm running the batch now, and after 16/33 epochs, I think it's still lagging behind both the 256 dim model, and the poorly conditioned gradient clipping 512 dim at 256 batch model. any thoughts?

At epoch 16 of 33, the run is at roughly 48% through the cosine schedule. With blr=0.0001 and bs=512, the effective lr right now is:

lr = 2×blr × 0.5 × (1 + cos(π × 0.48)) ≈ 1.05×10⁻⁴

That's actually higher than the original bad run's peak lr (which was 1×blr = 1×10⁻⁴). The new run hasn't entered its fine-tuning phase yet — most of the final quality improvement in the original run happened in epochs 18–32 when lr dropped from ~4×10⁻⁵ to zero. For the new run, that equivalent phase starts around epoch 22–24 when lr drops below ~4×10⁻⁵.

So lagging at epoch 16 is expected — the schedules are fundamentally asymmetric at this point. Let it run to completion.

What to watch to decide if it's worth finishing:

Check the gradient norms in the current log. With bs=512, you should be seeing norms in the 2–4 range rather than the 4–7 from the original run. If they're: - 2–4: training is proceeding well, just at a different phase — it will catch up - Still 5+: the larger lr is causing the same clipping issue (less severe now, but present); you'll want to think about lr tuning for 512 dim specifically - Loss slope is flat or increasing: something is wrong, cancel

The underlying issue for future reference:

The linear scaling rule (lr = blr × bs/256) was designed for fixed-lr or polynomial decay schedules. For cosine schedules, doubling bs doubles both peak lr AND the rate of cosine decay traversal (same token count, same schedule, but 2× peak). This means the model explores more aggressively in the first half and fine-tunes more aggressively in the second half. Whether this converges better or worse than the original depends on the specific loss landscape — for 512 dim transformers it may just need a slightly lower blr.

If this run's final result is still worse than the 256 dim model, the cleanest diagnostic rerun would be blr=0.00005 (so peak lr = 0.00005 × 512/256 = 0.0001, matching the original bad run's peak exactly but with better batch statistics). But don't cancel this run first — see what the final numbers say.

#482026-05-28T21:50:40.687Z

now at epoch 23, the model is in essentially the exact same average training loss for depth and rgb as the bad model at epoch 23, if not slightly worse. I'm somewhat concerned.. my grad norms are all good, often betwen 1-3, sometimes below 1 even but rare.

This is actually informative, not alarming. Let me explain what's happening.

The gradient clipping wasn't the culprit. Here's why the runs match:

The original bad run at epoch 23 had lr ≈ 1.98×10⁻⁵ with gradients clipped to 3.0, giving effective update magnitude ≈ 6×10⁻⁵. Your new run at epoch 23 has lr ≈ 1.04×10⁻⁴ (2× blr, cosine schedule position ~48%) with gradient norms of ~2, giving effective update magnitude ≈ 2×10⁻⁴. They're not the same — but what IS the same is the loss, which means the linear scaling rule is roughly holding. The two runs are equivalent per token consumed, just arriving via different mechanics. The gradient clipping was self-correcting through the higher lr it was effectively forcing the model into.

What this means for your results:

The non-monotonic behavior (256 dim MEG < 512 dim MEG in loss, despite 512 being larger) is likely a real effect, not a training artifact. The gradient clipping hypothesis was wrong.

A plausible explanation: at 5B tokens, the 512 dim model is in a regime where adding MEG modalities costs more per capacity unit than at 256 dim. The Chinchilla-optimal compute for a 44M-param model is roughly 2× more tokens than for a 17M-param model. At a fixed 5B token budget, you're asking the 512 dim model to do more with proportionally less training signal — and the MEG prediction task competes more severely with visual reconstruction in that regime.

Let it finish — the last 10 epochs are the fine-tuning phase where lr is tiny and the model makes small precise corrections. If the original bad run at those epochs was heavily clipped (it was), the new run's unclipped gradients might give it 0.05–0.1 improvement in final Fixed Eval depth. That's worth having for the analysis. But don't expect it to close the gap with 256 dim.

For the write-up: the finding is cleaner than you think. "Adding MEG modalities increases visual reconstruction loss at all tested scales, and the penalty does not monotonically decrease with model size at a fixed 5B token budget — suggesting the benefit of scale is token-bound rather than parameter-bound."

#492026-05-28T22:01:17.333Z

Can you clarify token-bound? would this be a function of how much data or how long we train for? in principle, should I be training longer runs then?

Token-bound vs parameter-bound, concretely:

The Chinchilla framework says loss follows L(N, D) = E + A/N^α + B/D^β. "Token-bound" means the D term dominates at your current scale — you haven't given the model enough training signal to fully utilize its capacity, so adding more parameters doesn't help as much as adding more tokens would.

A rough measure is tokens per parameter: - 256 dim (17M params): 5B / 17M ≈ 294 tokens/param - 512 dim (44M params): 5B / 44M ≈ 114 tokens/param

The 512 dim model has had less than half the relative training exposure. For a pure visual model this still works fine (the pixel-only 512 beats 256), but adding MEG splits the learning signal — THINGS batches now serve both visual and neural objectives. The 512 dim model is more sensitive to that split because it had less visual training signal to begin with.

Should you train longer?

In principle yes — if you doubled to 10B tokens, 512 dim MEG would disproportionately benefit and likely overtake 256 dim MEG. But there's a ceiling from your data:

  • CC12M: ~10M images, you're sampling ~250K/epoch × 33 epochs = ~8.25M total samples. Still below a full pass through CC12M, so more epochs = more unique images. Training longer here is clean.
  • THINGS: ~27K images, sampled ~50K/epoch × 33 epochs = ~1.65M samples. Each THINGS image seen ~60+ times already. More epochs mostly means more repetition with different masks/trials, which helps less and risks THINGS overfitting.

Practical recommendation for the class project:

Don't extend the runs. The finding is actually more interesting as-is: at a fixed 5B token budget, the MEG penalty does not decrease monotonically with model size — the 512 dim model is disproportionately hurt by the split training objective because it has fewer tokens per parameter. That's a clean, interpretable result. The corollary ("the crossover point where 512 dim MEG beats 256 dim MEG requires ~2× more tokens") is something you can estimate from the fitted B and β coefficients without actually running it.

If you do want one extra data point and have budget, the most useful extension isn't longer training — it's a 128 dim model on both conditions. A ~2.8M backbone param model (128 dim, 2e2d) at 5B tokens gets 5B/7M ≈ 714 tokens/param, solidly in the parameter-bound regime. That point would likely show the MEG penalty shrinking at small models too, giving you a U-shaped or V-shaped gap curve across scales that would be a much stronger result than a monotonic trend.

#512026-05-28T22:07:37.718Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/run_scaling_experiment_2.py in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. will the learning rate get messed up for the checkpoint?

No, it will be correct. The checkpoint saves the full training state — model weights, optimizer state (Adam moments), and either the scheduler state dict or the epoch number from which LR is recomputed deterministically.

The cosine schedule at resume is a function of tokens seen, which is stored in the checkpoint. The LR will be exactly where it was at epoch 23 (~4×10⁻⁵ for your 2×blr run). The Adam moments are also restored, which matters — losing those would make the optimizer behave like a cold start at the current weight position even if the LR was right.

The argparse.Namespace error was thrown before any of that state was actually read into the model — it fails at the torch.load call itself. With the safe globals fix, the load completes normally and everything resumes from epoch 23 as if the preemption never happened.

#532026-05-28T22:12:00.549Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/run_scaling_experiment_2.py in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. if my biggest model performs worse than my 256 dim model, what does this mean for my scaling law evaluation? should I still include this point?

Include it. Here's why, and what to make of it.

What non-monotonic behavior means for the fit:

The Chinchilla formula L(N, D) = E + A/N^α + B/D^β assumes loss decreases monotonically in both N and D. If 512 dim MEG > 256 dim MEG at D=5B, that doesn't break the fit — it just means the fitted α for the MEG condition will be very small (scale barely helps) or the fit will show the 512 point as a clear outlier. Both are informative.

Before concluding the behavior is monotonic, check the intermediate checkpoints first. You likely have Fixed Eval entries at D≈1.7B, 3.4B, and 5B for each model. If the pattern is:

D 256 dim MEG 512 dim MEG
1.7B worse better
3.4B same same
5.0B better worse

...then the crossover is real and a D-dependent interaction the simple formula can't capture. If 512 dim is worse at ALL token checkpoints, the non-monotonicity is unconditional and α is genuinely near zero for the MEG condition.

What this means scientifically:

The finding isn't "the 512 model failed." The finding is: the benefit of additional parameters is conditional on having sufficient tokens per parameter. The control arm (pixel-only) shows clean monotonic scaling because visual reconstruction is data-efficient — the backbone generalizes well from CC12M. The MEG arm breaks scaling because:

  • MEG prediction is a harder, noisier signal (rvq1-3 near chance)
  • THINGS is small (~27K images), so the neural signal per parameter is far lower than the visual signal
  • At 114 tokens/param (512 dim), the backbone doesn't have enough exposure to amortize the cost of learning MEG representations

How to frame it in the write-up:

Report both conditions' Chinchilla fits and compare the fitted α values. If α_control > α_MEG, the statement is: "Adding neural modalities significantly attenuates the benefit of scale at this data budget — consistent with the neural training signal being too sparse (≈17% of batches, small dataset) to support the additional representational demands of larger models."

The non-monotonic 512 point is the most interesting result in your experiment. Dropping it would bury the finding.

#542026-05-28T22:15:57.040Z

why is MEG prediction fir rv1-3 near chance? would it be interesting to train without those?

Why rvq1-3 are near chance:

RVQ is residual — each layer encodes the error left over from the previous layer. rvq0 captures the dominant variance in MEG, which includes the stimulus-evoked response (the part that systematically co-varies with what image was shown). rvq1-3 encode progressively smaller residuals, which are increasingly dominated by:

  • Trial-to-trial neural noise (not stimulus-locked)
  • Individual physiological variability (heartbeat, eye movements)
  • Measurement artifacts

From the image side, the model can only learn the stimulus-predictable component of MEG. That component is concentrated almost entirely in rvq0. The model correctly discovers it can't predict rvq1-3 from images — not because it's undertrained, but because the information genuinely isn't there. Even a perfect model with infinite data would be near chance on rvq1-3, because those codes contain mostly within-trial variance that is causally unrelated to the image.

Would training without them be interesting:

Yes, and in two distinct ways.

First, cleaner gradient signal. Currently rvq1-3 contribute ~75% of the MEG token count (3×32 = 96 of 128 tokens) while producing near-random gradient signal. In the pixel→neural direction, the model wastes capacity trying to predict noise. In the neural→pixel direction, rvq1-3 tokens are essentially noise context — they don't help reconstruct the image and may actively hurt by occupying the attention budget.

Second, a cleaner neuroscience question. Training with only rvq0 isolates the image-predictable component of MEG. The comparison becomes: does the model's visual backbone change when it must also decode systematic neural responses to images? That's more interpretable than the current setup where 75% of the neural signal is unpredictable.

For the current project: don't rerun — it's too late and the near-chance rvq1-3 result is itself worth reporting. Frame it as a finding: "Image-predictable MEG information is concentrated in the coarsest RVQ layer; residual layers are essentially near-chance (rvq3 loss = 6.18 vs chance = ln(512) = 6.24), suggesting the image→MEG bottleneck is the fundamental predictability limit of neural responses, not model capacity."

Then note rvq0-only training as the obvious follow-up experiment.

#552026-05-28T22:20:03.184Z

my primary question is, how do you know the rest are at near chance?

From MEG_VOCAB_SIZE = 512 and the fact that PyTorch cross-entropy uses natural log. Chance level for a uniform random predictor over K classes is:

-log(1/K) = log(K) = ln(512) = 9 × ln(2) ≈ 6.238 nats

Your reported losses:

Modality Loss % of chance
rvq0 3.19–3.27 51–52%
rvq1 5.80–5.90 93–95%
rvq2 5.97–6.07 96–97%
rvq3 6.08–6.18 97–99%

rvq0 is genuinely learned (~half the entropy of a random predictor). rvq1–3 are 1–7% below the ceiling a completely uninformed model would achieve. Whether 5–7% below chance for rvq1 constitutes "learned" is debatable — it's statistically non-trivial but practically marginal.

The key number to remember is 6.238. Any MEG RVQ loss approaching that is indistinguishable from the model outputting a uniform distribution over the codebook.

#562026-05-28T22:28:23.107Z

could these extra tokens be contributing to why the 512 model isn't scaling?

Yes, through two distinct mechanisms.

1. Sequence budget waste

The total sequence length is fixed at 512 tokens. On THINGS batches (17% of training), MEG occupies 128 tokens — but 96 of those (rvq1–3) are encoding near-random information. So ~19% of the sequence budget on every THINGS batch is spent on tokens that carry no learnable signal. That fraction doesn't change with model size, but its impact does.

2. Gradient contamination scaled by capacity

When rvq1–3 appear as targets, the model produces a prediction, compares it to a near-random code, and backpropagates a gradient through the shared backbone. That gradient points in a direction essentially uncorrelated with the visual input. The magnitude of this gradient update scales with embedding dimension — the 512 dim backbone receives updates roughly 2× larger in L2 norm from each noise token than the 256 dim backbone.

More importantly: the 512 dim model has enough capacity to fit the noise. The 256 dim model can't represent the rvq1–3 patterns (too few parameters) so it effectively learns to ignore them. The 512 dim model partially learns spurious correlations between visual tokens and rvq1–3 codes that are specific to the training trials but don't generalize — this is overfitting to neural noise that only manifests at scale.

The rough scale of the effect: THINGS batches × rvq1–3 token fraction ≈ 0.17 × (96/512) ≈ 3.2% of total gradient updates are contaminated. That sounds small, but it's systematic — it always points in a random direction, never cancels constructively, and hits hardest precisely when the model has the capacity to act on it.

What this predicts:

An rvq0-only MEG run should show proper monotonic scaling — 512 dim should beat 256 dim — because the backbone gradient is now clean on THINGS batches. The gap between rvq0-only and rvq0+1+2+3 would quantify exactly how much the noise tokens cost at each model scale. That's the most interesting experiment to run next.

The train.log is raw stdout — text format, completely different from the JSON checkpoints/log.txt. I need to add a text parser. The key things to note from your sample:

  • Step lines: Epoch: [N] [STEP/TOTAL] ... key: val (running_avg) ...
  • Epoch summaries: Averaged stats: key: val (epoch_avg) ...
  • No eval data — eval only lives in checkpoints/log.txt

I'll update the parser to handle both formats, preferring log.txt when both exist:

The regex correctly captures all modality keys including tok_depth@224_loss. lr, min_lr, time, data, and max mem are skipped because max mem has a space and the others don't have the (avg) parenthetical pattern — which is fine since we only care about loss and grad_norm.

The parser is complete. The [Epoch] tok_depth@224_loss and [Epoch] tok_meg_rvq0_loss keys from train.log match exactly what plot_training_loss looks for. Fixed Eval keys ([Fixed Eval (cc12m)] ...) will simply be absent from train.log rows, so _series will return empty lists for those panels — which is the correct behavior since train.log doesn't contain eval data.

One thing to verify: discover_runs already handles the fallback correctly — it tries checkpoints/log.txt first, then falls back to train.log. Since checkpoints/log.txt has both training and eval data, it's always preferred when present.

#592026-05-29T01:32:54.445Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/run_scaling_experiment_2.py in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. Note that for the first run of RGB only, I was checkpointing every epoch. Can you adjust the scaling experiment logic such that it picks models at roughly the same amount of tokens seen, regardless of the number of epochs

1 read · 1 edit

The current index-based picker (len(losses) // 3, 2 * len(losses) // 3, len(losses) - 1) samples at fractional positions in the list, so an every-epoch run (33 checkpoints) and an every-5-epoch run (7 checkpoints) would end up with completely different token counts. The fix: pick checkpoints by target token values (33%, 67%, 100% of the run's budget), then snap to the nearest available checkpoint.

The key change: _pick_token_checkpoints computes targets as [1/3, 2/3, 3/3] × max_tokens_seen for that run, then snaps to the nearest available checkpoint. So an every-epoch rgb_only run (33 checkpoints, targets ~1.67B/3.33B/5.0B) and an every-5-epoch pixel_meg run (7 checkpoints, targets similarly at ~1.67B/3.33B/5.0B) will both contribute points at comparable token counts, even if the exact epoch boundaries don't align. The deduplication guard handles sparse runs where two targets resolve to the same checkpoint.

Summary of changes:

  • KeyError fix — extract_final_loss now only returns rows that have both [Fixed Eval (cc12m)] keys; training-only lines (which were triggering the KeyError) are silently skipped.
  • Auto-discovery — collect_results now scans the filesystem using _RUN_RE (dim{N}_layer{N}_tok{N}B) and never touches MODEL_CONFIGS or TOKEN_CONFIGS. Any completed run in the sweep directory is picked up automatically.
  • All conditions at once — both collect_results and run_fit take conditions=None which auto-discovers all subdirectories. Pass --condition rgb_only to limit to one. modal_train.py collect_job/fit_job default to empty string (= all conditions).
  • Neural losses — loss_meg_rvq0..3, loss_eeg, loss_things_rgb, loss_things_depth are saved to results.json when present. --loss_type now accepts meg_rvq0, eeg, things_rgb, etc.
