#662026-05-29T05:45:59.700Z

[Image: original 2100x856, displayed at 2000x815. Multiply coordinates by 1.05 to map to original image.]

The plot tells a clean story. A few things to parse:

Why the 512 dim model underperforms, and what it isn't

The training and eval curves are nearly on top of each other throughout, so classical overfitting (train loss << eval loss) is not happening. The model generalizes to the val set just as well as it fits the training set — the problem is that both are converging to a higher loss than the 256 dim model.

The more precise framing: at 5B tokens, 512 dim has ~114 tokens/param versus ~294 for 256 dim. It's compute-suboptimal — it hasn't seen enough data to fully train its capacity. This is exactly what Chinchilla scaling predicts: for every model size there's an optimal token budget, and below that budget the model is underfitted relative to its capacity, not overfitted.

On "saturating the embedding space with noise"

Your intuition is partially right, but the mechanism is subtler. It's not that the model wastes dimensions on EEG noise — if it had excess capacity, extra dimensions could in principle be orthogonalized away from the RGB/depth subspace. The actual problem is that all modalities compete for the same token budget (256 input + 256 output) and the same attention heads. A larger model doesn't get more tokens; it gets more parameters per token, which is only valuable if the data complexity justifies it. If EEG carries low-structure information, the marginal complexity added doesn't scale with N.

Should you fit the scaling law including the 512 dim point?

Yes — and you should include it, not exclude it. Here's why: excluding the 512 dim point to get a cleaner monotonic curve would be fitting a law that says "larger models always help at fixed 5B tokens," which is simply false. The Chinchilla formula L(N, D) = E + A/N^α + B/D^β actually handles this correctly — with both N and D variation (you have 3 checkpoint token counts per model, so you do have D variation), the fit will find parameters that predict 512 dim at 5B tokens correctly as overcapacity.

What the law will tell you is the scaling exponent α for EEG, and whether it differs from the control arm. That's the scientifically interesting result. You can then add a sentence noting that the dim=512 model appears to be above the compute-optimal N for D=5B — this is a finding, not a confound to sweep under the rug.

The honest framing for the paper: "At fixed D=5B tokens, loss decreases with N up to dim=256, consistent with Chinchilla compute-optimal scaling; dim=512 exceeds the optimal model size for this token budget."

#672026-05-29T05:51:36.417Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/cfgs/neural/4m/modal/model/4m-neural-3e-3d-scaling.yaml in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. the input attention heads are fixed? aren't there multiple encoders and decoders, one per modality? also can I increase the number of input and target tokens?

num_heads is for the shared backbone, not per-modality

4M does not have one encoder/decoder per modality. The architecture is: - One shared encoder (2 or 3 transformer layers of self-attention) - One shared decoder (2 or 3 layers of cross-attention + self-attention) - Per-modality lookup tables: each modality has its own encoder embedding (vocab × dim) and decoder input/output embedding - Per-modality output heads: a small linear projection per modality from dim → vocab

num_heads is the number of attention heads in the single shared transformer. The constraint head_dim = dim / num_heads = 64 (so dim=256 → 4 heads, dim=512 → 8 heads) is just to keep each head's representational capacity fixed as you scale up. None of this is per-modality.

The modality-specific pieces — the embedding tables and output heads — are what make different modalities representable. The transformer itself is completely shared, which is why find_unused_params: true matters: when a batch has no EEG samples, the EEG embedding and head get zero gradient, but DDP still needs to know to sync them.

On increasing num_input_tokens / num_target_tokens

Yes, these are configurable. At patch_size: 16, input_size: 224: - RGB or depth: 14×14 = 196 tokens each - MEG rvq0–3: 32 tokens each (128 total for all 4 layers) - EEG: likely 32 tokens

So your full multimodal budget is 196+196+128 = 520 tokens for MEG, or 196+196+32 = 424 for EEG. With 256 input + 256 target = 512 slots, you're already slightly undersized for the full MEG case — some tokens get dropped or the masking alpha is doing heavy selection.

The practical cost of increasing: - Attention is O(sequence²), so 384→512 input tokens raises attention memory ~78% - For your A100-40GB budget at dim=512, you're already running bs=256 to fit memory — increasing tokens would require reducing batch size further

For your current experiments it's probably not worth it: EEG at 32 tokens fits comfortably in 256, and the Dirichlet masking already handles the case where not all modality tokens appear. If you were to add higher-resolution modalities (448×448 → 784 tokens per image) it would become necessary.

#682026-05-29T06:08:07.842Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/run_scaling_experiment_2.py in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. you mention how the modalities might be fighting for the same attention heads. but the large model has more capacity right, in terms of more attention heads and whatnot? is the issue for the larger model not enough data diversity then? that is, even training on more tokens, it wouldn't improve much? or that it needs more tokens? can you explain the relation between training with more tokens and training with more diverse data, and what is happening in this case? lastly, for cc12m, there is roughy 10x more data. for the neural models, I weighted more heavily toward the THINGS dataset with matching neural recordings. do you think I should undo this reweighting? could that help with more diversity? what is the relationship between weighting datasets and weighting modalities, especially when some datasets lack certain modalities?

you mention how the modalities might be fighting for the same attention heads. but the large model has more capacity right, in terms of more attention heads and whatnot? is the issue for the larger model not enough data diversity then? that is, even training on more tokens, it wouldn't improve much? or that it needs more tokens? can you explain the relation between training with more tokens and training with more diverse data, and what is happening in this case? lastly, for cc12m, there is roughy 10x more data. for the neural models, I weighted more heavily toward the THINGS dataset with matching neural recordings. do you think I should undo this reweighting? could that help with more diversity? what is the relationship between weighting datasets and weighting modalities, especially when some datasets lack certain modalities?

Capacity and attention heads in the larger model

Yes, dim=512 has 8 heads vs 4, and each head has the same 64-dim capacity, so in principle there's more room to learn independent subspaces for visual and neural features. The bottleneck isn't representation capacity — it's that the model hasn't received enough gradient signal to use that capacity well. At 5B tokens and 44M params, the model is compute-suboptimal: it has initialized 44M parameters but the training signal has only partially shaped them. The 17M model at the same token count is much more "full" relative to its capacity.

Data diversity vs. more tokens — what's actually different

These are genuinely distinct:

  • More tokens at fixed dataset size = more epochs over the same images. For your ~300K CC12M + ~26K THINGS, 5B tokens is already ~27 passes through CC12M and ~63 passes through THINGS (due to the 5:1 weighting). Additional passes over identical images give diminishing returns because the model has already learned what those images have to teach. Gradient updates become small and redundant.

  • More diverse data = new images the model hasn't seen, providing genuinely novel gradient signal. Each new image contributes information the model can't have memorized.

For the 512 dim model specifically: if you trained it to ~20B tokens, it would reach roughly the same tokens/param ratio as 256 dim at 5B tokens, and would likely surpass it on CC12M fixed eval. But — with only 300K CC12M images and 26K THINGS images, a 50M parameter model will start memorizing specific image patterns well before 20B tokens. The data diversity ceiling means the practical compute-optimal point is lower than you'd predict from a pure Chinchilla calculation on an unlimited dataset. So it's both: the model needs more tokens and it would benefit from more diverse data, and at large N the diversity wall hits harder than at small N.

The reweighting confound

This is worth thinking about carefully because it creates a comparison confound. Your setups:

Condition CC12M weight THINGS weight CC12M fraction
rgb_only 9.0 1.0 90%
pixel_meg 5.0 1.0 83%
pixel_eeg 5.0 1.0 83%

The control arm sees ~10% more CC12M per step. Since CC12M is your fixed eval dataset, this means the neural arm is slightly disadvantaged on CC12M eval purely by weighting — independently of whether neural modalities help or hurt visual learning. For a class project this is probably a minor effect, but it's worth acknowledging.

Dataset weight vs. modality weight — the relationship

These operate at different levels:

  • Dataset weight ([5.0, 1.0]) controls the proportion of batches drawn from each dataset. THINGS batches include neural tokens; CC12M batches don't. So increasing THINGS weight directly increases how many gradient steps the neural encoder/decoder heads receive per epoch.

  • Alpha config controls, within a THINGS batch, what appears in the input vs. target context — the direction of prediction. This doesn't change how often the neural heads are trained, only the direction (pixel→neural vs neural→pixel vs all2all).

The interaction: CC12M batches train the visual components only (the MEG/EEG heads get zero gradient — this is why find_unused_params: true is critical). So the ratio of THINGS:CC12M batches is essentially the ratio of "neural gradient steps : visual-only gradient steps." Weighting heavily toward CC12M starves the neural heads but enriches visual representations.

Should you undo the reweighting?

For the neural arms, I'd keep [5.0, 1.0] as-is. The motivation for boosting THINGS is sound — if you dropped to [9.0, 1.0], the neural heads would see only ~1/10 of batches and training signal for neural prediction would be very thin. The confound with CC12M eval is real but interpretable: just note in the write-up that the neural conditions see slightly less CC12M per step, and frame the CC12M eval as "somewhat conservative" for the neural arm.

What might actually help more: training the rgb_only_pure_all2all control condition with [5.0, 1.0] weights to match the neural arm exactly, so the only difference is whether THINGS batches carry neural tokens. That's the cleanest apples-to-apples comparison. You'd be running rgb_only_pure_all2all with the same dataset mix as the neural arm.

#702026-05-29T07:26:04.269Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/cfgs/neural/4m/modal/data/rgb-depth-pure-all2all.yaml in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. modal just preempted my process in the middle of a validation, just after epoch 14 where a checkpoint was made. will it still run validation if I restart it? or should I just delete the latest checkpoint and retrain those 5 epochs

Just restart — don't delete. Here's the reasoning:

auto_resume=True (the default) will load the epoch 14 checkpoint and resume from there. Training continues forward: epoch 15, 16, ... . Validation isn't re-run retroactively for the checkpoint you resume from, but that's fine — the interrupted validation (if it was a mid-epoch-15-eval preemption) simply wasn't written to log.txt, and the epoch 15 eval will run normally once epoch 15 training finishes.

The only case where you'd want to delete is if the checkpoint file itself got corrupted because preemption happened during the write of the checkpoint. Since you said it was "just after" epoch 14's checkpoint completed, the file should be intact. You can verify quickly:

# check the file is non-zero and not truncated
ls -lh /project/data/scaling_sweep/<condition>/dim<N>_layer<L>_tok<T>/checkpoints/checkpoint-14.pth

If the size looks reasonable (not 0 bytes), just restart and it'll pick up cleanly.

#712026-05-29T07:28:04.863Z

if the log is missing a validation step, will that mess with the plotting code?

No, it's handled gracefully. Both the collection and plotting code just skip rows that don't have eval keys — they never assume a fixed number of checkpoints.

Specifically: - extract_final_loss filters rows to those that contain both _CC12M_REQUIRED keys; training-only rows are silently skipped, so a missing validation step just means one fewer data point. - _pick_token_checkpoints snaps to the nearest available checkpoint at 1/3, 2/3, 3/3 of the token budget, so it adapts to whatever rows exist. - _series in plot_training.py just collects (tokens, value) pairs from whatever rows are present — no fixed-length assumptions.

With eval_freq=5 over 33 epochs you'd normally have ~6 eval points; losing one still leaves 5, which is well above the 3-point minimum Chinchilla needs.

Here are the scaling law fits for the default conditions. This is for cc12m fixed eval.

These are genuinely interesting results. Let me walk through what they say.

Depth loss fits (most diagnostic for neural modalities):

Condition E (irreducible) A α (N) MAE
rgb_only 7.293 22.5 0.31 0.023
pixel_meg 7.397 1021 0.56 0.148
pixel_eeg 7.636 8199 0.82 0.139

Three things stand out:

1. E increases with neural complexity. The irreducible lower bound rises by +0.10 nats (MEG) and +0.34 nats (EEG) for depth. This is the "at infinite scale, neural conditions still lose" signal — the model is trading depth representation quality to accommodate neural reconstruction. EEG is worse than MEG, which is consistent with EEG being spatially noisier and taking up representational capacity without contributing useful structure.

2. A is enormous for neural conditions. A encodes how far above the irreducible loss you are due to finite model size. rgb_only's A=22.5 means it's nearly converged at the model sizes you ran. pixel_meg's A=1021 and pixel_eeg's A=8199 mean the neural models are far from their scaling floor — they'd need substantially larger models to reach the equivalent point. The higher α compensates somewhat (steeper improvement per parameter), but the starting gap is large.

3. Fit quality degrades. rgb_only's MAE of 0.023 relative to a loss range of ~0.28 is clean (~8%). pixel_meg/eeg depth MAE of ~0.14 on a range of ~0.48 is ~29%. This means the Chinchilla fit is extrapolating more than fitting, and the inferred E/A/α values carry significant uncertainty. You probably don't have enough models in the sweet spot of the scaling curve for those conditions — the three models you have may all be in the steep part of the A/N^α term, so the optimizer is fitting a slope but can't pin down E reliably.

RGB loss tells a similar story but more muted. The E gap is smaller (rgb_only 8.687, pixel_meg 8.720, pixel_eeg 8.905), which makes sense — RGB reconstruction doesn't compete with neural signals as directly as depth does. EEG still costs 0.22 nats of irreducible RGB loss, which is surprisingly large.

Bottom line: The core result is there — neural modalities incur a consistent loss penalty at all scales, and EEG hurts more than MEG. But the large A values and poor depth fit quality for neural conditions suggest your models are all in the data-starved / parameter-starved regime for those conditions, so the extrapolated E values should be treated as rough estimates rather than clean predictions. The comparison holds directionally; the magnitudes are uncertain.

One thing worth checking: does the gap close or widen as model size increases? That would distinguish "task interference that scale can fix" (narrowing gap) from "fundamentally harder task" (stable or widening gap). The condition_gap_{treatment}.png plots you just added will show this directly.

#732026-05-29T07:47:39.740Z

do you have any suggestions on what I can reasonably do for my neural arm models?

I pasted two images, the first one is my MEG arm on depth, and the second is my control arm on RGB. interestingly enough, I don't see the efficient frontier line on the control arm which in theory is a better fit no?

Two separate questions, both have interesting answers.

Why the efficient frontier is missing/faint on the control RGB plot:

This is actually a signal, not a bug. For rgb_only RGB, A=16.24 is tiny — at your largest model (22M params), the parameter term A/N^α = 16.24/22M^0.40 ≈ 0.015 nats above E. The model has essentially converged to the irreducible loss floor.

The efficient frontier position is set by N/D = (αA)/(βB) = (0.40×16.24)/(0.31×224) ≈ 0.094. That means optimal is D ≈ 10.6N tokens-per-parameter. Your runs are at D/N ≈ 1500–5000 — you're running 200–500× more tokens than optimal for these model sizes. The efficient frontier line sits far to the right of your data points, outside the axis bounds. The control RGB task is basically solved at these scales, which is why the contours are so tight.

For MEG depth, A=1021 is large, the models are far from E, and the frontier passes through your data range — hence it's visible.

What you can reasonably do for the neural arm:

The core problem is all your neural arm models are in the steep part of the curve (far from their E), so the Chinchilla fit is extrapolating rather than interpolating. Three options in decreasing cost:

  1. Add one dim=512 MEG run. This extends N by ~4× and would pull α toward a more stable estimate. It's the single highest-leverage thing — pinning the top end of the parameter range dramatically reduces uncertainty on the A/N^α fit. One run ≈ 2 GPU hours.

  2. Reframe the analysis around the gap plots. Rather than trusting the absolute Chinchilla extrapolation for neural conditions, the treatment−control gap at matched (N, D) is more reliable because systematic biases cancel. The directional finding — neural modalities cost a consistent loss penalty that doesn't close with scale — is clear even if the extrapolated E values have wide error bars. This is a legitimate methodological choice for a class project.

  3. Fix α and β to canonical values. Constrain α=0.34, β=0.28 (from the original Chinchilla paper) and only fit A, B, E. With 9 points this reduces overfitting and gives more stable E estimates, at the cost of assuming the neural arm scales like language models. The fit quality (MAE) would likely worsen but the reported parameters would be less sensitive to noise.

I'd probably do (1) for MEG if budget allows, and (2) as the primary argument regardless. The gap plot interpretation is more honest about the data limitations anyway.

#742026-05-29T07:52:18.299Z

I do have a dim=512 run in MEG.

My mistake — the 12-point fit already has dim=512 included. 22,020,096 = (12+16) × 3 × 512², so it's the dim512_layer6 run that's already in the collection.

That changes the diagnosis. The noisy fit isn't a data coverage problem — you have 4 model sizes spanning 24× in N, which is actually decent. The issue is that all 4 models sit far from their irreducible loss E, so the optimizer has to extrapolate E from the steep part of the curve rather than anchoring on a near-converged model. Given that, the most practical thing for a class project is probably to sidestep the noisy 5-parameter fit entirely and just present a simpler fixed-D power law: at each condition's final token checkpoint (~5B tokens), fit L = c + a·N^(-α) across the 4 model sizes. This has 3 parameters, 4 points, and directly answers "how does loss scale with model size at matched training budget" — which is actually the cleanest framing for your comparison question anyway.

The gap plots (treatment − control at matched N and D) are still the most honest comparison regardless of which fit you use, since the systematic biases from being off the efficient frontier cancel out.

#752026-05-29T08:23:31.454Z

ok, I can certainly try that, let me know if Chinchilla's scaling supports that, and how I might try to fit fewer parameter scaling laws. I still want to get a cleaner understanding of the model scaling and why there is a gap. ok, I can certainly try that, let me know if Chinchilla's scaling supports that, and how I might try to fit fewer parameter scaling laws. I still want to get a cleaner understanding of the model scaling and why there is a gap. For the largest model with EEG, the performance is the worst of all models, whereas for MEG, it is second place often. any thoughts on why this might be? additionally, if I were to train the 512 dim model up to 20B tokens seen, do you expect the performance to converge to that of the 256 dim model? that way, the tokens per parameter would match

Three separate questions — let me take them in order.

Simpler fit with scipy:

The chinchilla library only does the full 5-parameter L(N,D) fit. For a fixed-D power law you'd do it outside:

from scipy.optimize import curve_fit
import numpy as np

def power_law(N, E, A, alpha):
    return E + A / N**alpha

# Collect (N, loss) at the final token checkpoint per model
N_vals = np.array([917504, 2064384, 5505024, 22020096])  # dim128,192,256,512
L_vals = np.array([...])  # final-checkpoint losses from results.json

popt, pcov = curve_fit(power_law, N_vals, L_vals,
                       p0=[7.0, 100.0, 0.4], maxfev=10000,
                       bounds=([6.0, 0, 0.01], [10.0, 1e6, 2.0]))
E, A, alpha = popt

With 4 points and 3 parameters you only have 1 degree of freedom, so E will be poorly constrained. The most useful output is just α (the N-scaling exponent) and a comparison of α across conditions — that directly answers "does adding neural data change how loss scales with model size?"

Why dim=512 is worst for EEG but only second for MEG:

EEG is much lower SNR than MEG — fewer sensors, worse spatial resolution, more muscle/eye artifact. A 22M-parameter model has enough capacity to memorize idiosyncratic noise patterns in the ~30K paired EEG training samples. Because the encoder is shared, these noise-fitting activations compete with the depth/RGB representations in the attention heads. The result is the large model actively degrades the shared representation more than the small model does.

MEG has enough coherent spatial structure across its ~300 channels that the 22M model doesn't fully memorize noise — it's learning something real, even if imperfectly. Hence it's second rather than last. The E gap in the fits confirms this: pixel_eeg irreducible loss for depth is 7.636 vs pixel_meg's 7.397 — EEG inflicts a much larger permanent penalty on depth reconstruction even at infinite scale.

Training dim=512 to 20B tokens:

The equal-tokens-per-param matching doesn't actually place two models at the same loss level. Larger models are more sample-efficient — they reach a given loss in fewer tokens, not the same number. The correct matching criterion is the iso-loss contour: find D₂ such that L(22M, D₂) = L(5.5M, 5B).

For rgb_only with the fitted parameters, the calculation gives D₂ ≈ 3.8B tokens — meaning dim=512 matches dim=256@5B after only ~3.8B tokens of training, not 20B. Training to 20B would put dim=512 significantly ahead of dim=256, not at the same point.

For MEG the fit is noisier but the same logic holds. Training dim=512 to 20B tokens won't help you understand the convergence question — it would just tell you that the larger model continues improving, which you already know.

The more useful experiment (if you have budget) would be training dim=256 and dim=512 to the same compute budget (same FLOPs = 6ND), which places both on comparable footing under Chinchilla theory. But honestly, for the class project the fixed-D α comparison at matched token counts is probably sufficient.

#762026-05-29T09:21:04.733Z

The user selected the lines 153 to 153 from /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/run_scaling_experiment_2.py: large_gpu

This may or may not be related to the current task. Since we are repeating tokens, it appears that Chinchilla scaling may be the wrong tool to use - rather, I should be using the Muennighoff correction right? Do you know if there is code that can calculate that?

Yes, you're right — the standard Chinchilla formula assumes each token is unique, and at 33 epochs over 153M unique visual tokens you're deeply in the repeated-data regime. The relevant paper is Muennighoff et al. 2023, "Scaling Data-Constrained Language Models" (arXiv 2305.16264). Their key finding is that repeated tokens are worth progressively less than fresh tokens, and beyond ~4 repetitions the marginal benefit drops sharply.

Their correction replaces raw D with an effective token count D_eff that grows sublinearly in the number of repetitions R = D/U. The reference implementation (and curve-fitting code) is in the HuggingFace datablations repo that accompanies the paper — search for it on GitHub. It includes the fitted constants for the correction.

A few things worth noting for your specific case:

The correction is worse for neural modalities than visual. With [5.0, 1.0] weights and THINGS having ~26K images × 512 tokens ≈ 13M unique THINGS tokens, THINGS contributes ~17% of batches → ~835M THINGS tokens seen total → ~64 repetitions for the neural data, versus ~33 for CC12M. The Muennighoff D_eff for MEG/EEG will be substantially lower than the raw 5B, making the effective data budget for neural modalities even smaller than it looks.

Cross-condition comparisons are mostly unaffected. The Muennighoff correction shifts all conditions' D axes, but since both control and treatment use the same CC12M visual data with the same repetition rate, the depth/RGB gap between conditions is still valid. What changes is the absolute value of D on the x-axis and consequently the fitted β — the N-scaling exponent α you'd get from the fixed-D power law fit is untouched.

For a class project, I'd suggest: (1) note the repeated-data limitation explicitly, (2) apply the Muennighoff D_eff correction to the x-axis before fitting, (3) use the corrected D in the Chinchilla fit so the B/β parameters aren't biased. The datablations repo has self-contained fitting code you can adapt. The α comparison across conditions remains your cleanest result regardless.

#772026-05-29T17:44:24.282Z

Lastly, my neural validation loss ends up increasing with tokens seen, and there is always a decent gap compared to the training, indicating overfitting. how much of this effect is down to validation always occuring on the first trial, while training on random selections? it's interesting that it's able to learn a signal because training loss decreases.

Lastly, my neural validation loss ends up increasing with tokens seen, and there is always a decent gap compared to the training, indicating overfitting. how much of this effect is down to validation always occuring on the first trial, while training on random selections? it's interesting that it's able to learn a signal because training loss decreases.

The trial selection asymmetry explains a fixed offset in the gap, not the increasing validation loss — those are two separate effects worth disentangling.

What trial selection does: If training randomly samples trials while validation is fixed to trial 0, the expected validation loss at epoch 1 is already slightly higher than training loss, because validation gets one specific noise realization while the training average is over the whole trial distribution. But this offset is roughly constant — it doesn't grow over epochs. It's essentially measuring E[loss on trial 0] vs E[loss on random trial], which should be similar if the neural noise is roughly i.i.d. across trials.

What's causing the increasing validation loss: That's genuine overfitting to trial-specific noise in the training set. The model is being supervised on individual noisy trials (each pass) and it has enough capacity to memorize the noise patterns specific to those trials — learning to predict idiosyncratic noise artifacts rather than the stable evoked response. The shared encoder is gradually encoding noise-specific features that don't transfer to unseen trial 0.

The fact that training loss decreases tells you the signal is real. The model is capturing E[neural response | image] — the systematic structure across images — and the decreasing loss means it's doing so successfully. The overfitting just means it's also memorizing noise on top of that, which hurts generalization.

The cleanest fix in retrospect would have been to use the average across trials as the training target rather than individual trials — that would make training supervision match validation supervision, and remove the noise memorization pathway entirely. With averaged targets, the model can only learn the stable signal component, and the train/val gap should close. Worth noting as a limitation: the gap you see is partly fundamental (single-trial noise) and partly architectural (too many parameters for the amount of truly unique signal in 26K × T trials).

11 edit · 6 read · 1 bash · 1 write

Good questions — let me address both before writing any code.

Trial sampling and batch shuffle interaction:

When the dataloader constructs a sample it first picks one trial randomly from image A's recordings via MegTokTransform/EegTokTransform. That single-trial token is what lands in the batch. The batch-level shuffle then swaps those already-selected single-trial tokens across images — so after shuffling, image A receives one random trial from image B's recording. The trial selection and the shuffle are independent operations. This is actually ideal for the ablation: the shuffled target is maximally wrong (different image and random trial of that image), so any training signal that survives the shuffle would have to come from statistical regularities in the neural token distribution itself rather than from image-neural correspondence.

#822026-05-31T06:02:04.311Z

can you double check the implementation is correct? for some reason, the model is seeing very low training loss (0.6302) for the meg residual tokens. is this expected? surely I should expect training loss to be at random chance? also, this implementation of label randomization seems to cause the model to perform slightly better than without shuffled labels.

1 edit

can you double check the implementation is correct? for some reason, the model is seeing very low training loss (0.6302) for the meg residual tokens. is this expected? surely I should expect training loss to be at random chance? also, this implementation of label randomization seems to cause the model to perform slightly better than without shuffled labels.

The low loss and the shuffled-model-outperforming issue both point to the same bug: torch.randperm(B) shuffles across ALL batch samples including CC12M samples, which have zero-value placeholder MEG tokens. THINGS samples receiving those zero placeholders as targets can trivially predict them, driving loss down. And the shuffled model "improves" because it's no longer wasting capacity trying to fit real-but-noisy neural signals — it just predicts zeros for most MEG targets.

The fix: identify which samples have real neural data by checking the masks (placeholder samples have all tokens masked out, i.e., (~input_mask).any(dim=1) is False) and only permute within that subset.

The core change: instead of torch.randperm(B) over all batch samples, we now: 1. Find neural_idx — the subset of samples where (~input_mask).any() | (~target_mask).any() is True (i.e., at least one unmasked neural token exists) 2. Generate randperm(len(neural_idx)) over only those samples 3. Apply the permutation by cloning the tensor first, then writing shuffled values back only into neural_idx positions

With the old code, a batch of 64 had ~53 CC12M samples with zero placeholder tokens. Those zeros got shuffled into THINGS samples' target positions, making prediction trivial (loss → 0). The model was effectively learning "predict zeros for most MEG targets" and devoting freed capacity to visual reconstruction — which is why the shuffled model appeared better. After this fix, only the ~11 real THINGS samples per batch are shuffled among themselves, which is the correct ablation.

#832026-05-31T06:26:44.785Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/run_scaling_experiment_2.py in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. I added a new condition, pixel_meg_rvq0_shuffled to shuffle just labels. It's a simple change where I just use the same rvq0 configs and set shuffle_neural to true. It seems like the model is still learning to decrease the loss for rvq0 from 6 to 2.5 or so, maybe even less. if there's nothing wrong with the implementation, it could just be learning shared features across all meg rv0 features? what might this tell us about the tokenization process, if anything?

I added a new condition, pixel_meg_rvq0_shuffled to shuffle just labels. It's a simple change where I just use the same rvq0 configs and set shuffle_neural to true. It seems like the model is still learning to decrease the loss for rvq0 from 6 to 2.5 or so, maybe even less. if there's nothing wrong with the implementation, it could just be learning shared features across all meg rv0 features? what might this tell us about the tokenization process, if anything?

No implementation bug — this is a meaningful finding about the tokenizer. The decreasing loss with shuffled labels has two distinct sources:

1. Non-uniform codebook usage (pure marginal statistics)

Starting near log(1024) ≈ 6.93 and falling to ~2.5 means the rvq0 codebook entropy is ~2.5 nats — an effective vocabulary of only about e^2.5 ≈ 12 frequently-used codes out of 1024. The model learns to predict the dominant codes regardless of any input. This is a sign of codebook underutilization or collapse: the VQ-VAE encoder maps most MEG recordings to a small cluster of centroids in the primary codebook. This is common when VQ-VAE training data is limited or the codebook wasn't trained with diversity incentives (e.g., perplexity loss or exponential moving average updates with restarts).

2. Within-sample spatial correlations

The shuffle permutes tokens across samples but preserves the internal token arrangement within each recording. MEG sensors are spatially correlated — adjacent sensors measure overlapping magnetic fields — so rvq0 tokens at neighboring spatial positions are predictable from each other. The 4M attention mechanism can exploit this even when the labels are from the "wrong" image, because the spatial structure of sample B's tokens is intact. This allows the model to achieve loss below the marginal entropy, purely from the within-recording structure.

What this tells you about the tokenization

  • The rvq0 codes primarily encode a coarse, globally similar structure across MEG recordings (slow oscillations, common sensor patterns) rather than image-specific responses. There isn't much image-conditional information at the primary quantization level.
  • rvq1-3 should theoretically encode the residuals — the image-specific detail — but as you saw earlier those are near random-chance, suggesting the VQ-VAE doesn't successfully represent discriminative neural signal in higher residual levels. The interesting information (if any) is in rvq0, but it's buried under the dominant shared structure.

How to use this as a baseline

The shuffled validation loss asymptote is your empirical lower bound for "what can be predicted from token statistics alone." The gap between unshuffled val loss and shuffled val loss at matched tokens-seen is the cleanest estimate of the genuine image-conditional signal the model captures. A small or absent gap would mean the unshuffled model isn't learning image-neural correspondence — it's just learning the same marginal structure as the shuffled model.

#892026-06-03T04:42:55.250Z

I'm noticing decently strong overfitting in the neural modality, what can I adjust in the 4m architecture to help reduce this?

The core problem is that THINGS has ~26K images with no cross-epoch augmentation variety for tok_meg_avg (single averaged trial), while the decoder head is trained only on those samples. Four levers, ordered by impact-to-effort:

1. drop_path_rate — highest impact, one YAML line

Stochastic depth is the primary regularizer for ViT-style models and directly reduces memorization of the small THINGS set. The current scaling configs don't set it (defaults to 0). Add to all neural model configs:

drop_path: 0.1   # or 0.05 for smaller models

This stochastically drops entire residual blocks, forcing the model not to rely on fixed computation paths. Affects the whole backbone, which is what you want — the overfitting is in the shared weights the decoder reads from.

2. Reduce neural target alpha — less gradient from memorized tokens

In rgb-meg-avg-bidirectional-all2all.yaml (and the rvq0 variant), the all2all component uses target_alphas: 0.5 for neural modalities, meaning ~50% of cells are decoder targets on average. Drop this:

tok_meg_avg_rvq0:
  target_alphas: [0.5, 0., 0.3]   # was 0.5 in comp 2

Fewer neural targets per step → less opportunity to overfit the decoder head. Trade-off: slightly slower convergence on the neural signal.

3. Increase weight decay

Standard AdamW default (likely wd: 0.05 in 4M). The neural decoder head has few effective training samples. Add to model configs:

weight_decay: 0.1

Higher L2 on the shared weights discourages sharp fits to THINGS-specific patterns.

4. Increase wds_n_repeats for THINGS — more trial diversity per epoch

For regular MEG (non-avg), each repeat draws a different random trial, giving free augmentation. Currently wds_n_repeats: 2. Raising it to 4-6 in the THINGS dataset section of the data configs makes trial-sampling augmentation more effective per token-budget. For tok_meg_avg this does nothing (single trial), so levers 1-3 matter more there.

For pixel_meg_avg_rvq0 specifically, since there's zero trial variability, I'd start with drop_path: 0.1 — it's the only architectural lever that doesn't require touching the data pipeline or the alpha configs. If overfitting persists, the alpha reduction (#2) on top of that is the next most targeted fix.

9 bash · 1 read

No — drop_path_rate_encoder/drop_path_rate_decoder are parameters of fm.py:101-102, so the model supports them, but run_training_4m.py:379-385 calls create_model without passing them:

model = create_model(
    args.model,
    encoder_embeddings=encoder_embeddings,
    decoder_embeddings=decoder_embeddings,
    modality_info=modality_info,
    num_register_tokens=args.num_register_tokens,
    # drop_path_rate_encoder never passed → always 0.0
)

There's also no --drop_path_rate argparse argument in the trainer, so the YAML set_defaults path doesn't work either.

To actually use it you'd need to wire it up in lib/train_4m.py. The cleanest place is inside _drive_trainer_main (or wherever you hook into the model creation) — read drop_path_rate off trainer.args and patch the model after it's constructed, or patch run_training_4m.py's create_model call via the same monkey-patch mechanism you use for token accounting.

The practical alternative that requires no code changes: weight decay (weight_decay: 0.1 in the model YAML) is properly wired through argparse at run_training_4m.py:108 and does work directly from the config. That's the regularization knob you can actually turn today without touching Python.

2 bash · 2 read · 2 edit

Done. The two diagnostics are pre-existing unrelated hints, not caused by the edit.

You can now set it directly in any model YAML:

drop_path_rate_encoder: 0.1
drop_path_rate_decoder: 0.1

Suggested starting points: 0.05–0.1 for encoder, 0.1–0.2 for decoder. The decoder sees more gradient signal from the neural heads (which have high loss early on), so slightly higher stochastic depth there helps more. Keep encoder lower — it's shared across all modalities and over-regularizing it hurts visual reconstruction.

#952026-06-03T05:04:47.787Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/run_scaling_experiment_2.py in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. do you think it's worth sweeping the learning rate also based on the training trajectories

For a class project at this budget: no, not worth it.

The goal is to compare two conditions (with vs. without neural), not to locate optimal Chinchilla exponents precisely. As long as LR is held constant across conditions and model sizes, the relative effect of adding the neural modality on the scaling curve is still cleanly measurable — a LR bias would shift both arms equally.

Where it would matter: if you were trying to publish precise α/β exponents from the Chinchilla fit. There, a suboptimal LR at the largest model size suppresses loss and flattens the scaling slope. For showing "neural modality shifts the curve up/down by X nats," it doesn't change the conclusion.

The more actionable thing given what the trajectories are actually showing (overfitting in neural, not instability) is the drop_path_rate you just wired in. That's the right lever — LR sweeping would cost ~3–4× the budget for runs that don't change your story.

The one exception: if you see loss spikes or divergence in the 44M model specifically, that's a signal the current LR is too high at that scale, and you'd want to drop it by 2×. But that's a point fix on one run, not a sweep.

#962026-06-03T05:08:44.903Z

the big model (44-50m) in many cases performs worse in all categories - perhaps I should tune the learning rate? or is the model too big?

Most likely it's the learning rate, not model size. The "large model performs worse than smaller model" pattern almost always points to LR being too high relative to the model's width — wider models (dim=512 vs 256) have larger gradient magnitudes and the same blr hits harder on them.

The data story also contributes for the neural modality specifically: you have ~30K THINGS images with neural labels. The 44M model's neural decoder heads have significantly more parameters to fit from the same labeled pool, so neural loss being worse there is expected even if the LR were perfect.

What to try first:

Run the 44M models (both conditions) with blr: 0.00005 (half the current 0.0001). That's one re-run per condition — cheap, and if it closes the gap it confirms LR, not capacity.

Don't reduce it for all sizes. The 7M/11M/17M models are likely in a good regime already; changing their LR breaks your scaling curve comparability.

How to tell which cause it is:

  • If the 44M model's visual loss (tok_rgb, tok_depth) is also worse → LR is the dominant issue
  • If only neural loss is worse but visual is fine → it's the labeled data sparsity, and halving LR won't fix it

If it turns out to be the labeled data issue, the fix there is to increase the THINGS sampling weight (currently [5.0, 1.0] vs CC12M's 9.0) rather than touching LR.

#972026-06-03T05:13:00.771Z

the biggest model does perform best on pixel modalities only, but introducing neural data it struggles a lot

That pattern rules out LR — the backbone is fine, the neural heads specifically are struggling. This is the labeled data sparsity problem: ~30K THINGS images have neural labels, but the 44M model has proportionally more neural decoder capacity to train from the same pool.

Two levers, in order of ease:

1. Increase THINGS sampling weight (cheapest fix)

The 44M backbone gets gradient from 100% of batches (CC12M + THINGS). The neural heads only see gradient from ~10% of batches (THINGS only). For the 44M model, the backbone signal completely dominates. Raising the THINGS weight from 5.0 to something like 8.0–10.0 in the MEG/avg data configs gives the neural heads more relative gradient without changing anything structural:

weights: [9.0, 8.0]   # CC12M, THINGS — current is [9.0, 5.0] or [5.0, 1.0]

2. Higher drop_path_rate_decoder for the 44M model configs only

The neural heads are overfitting to 30K samples. The decoder is where they live, so regularizing it harder (e.g., drop_path_rate_decoder: 0.2) without touching the encoder would help the 44M case specifically without changing the smaller models.

Honest take for the class project:

The 44M model underperforming on neural but excelling on pixels is a finding — it says the neural modality creates a data bottleneck that breaks the scaling trend at larger model sizes. That's scientifically interesting and worth reporting as-is, rather than tuning it away. The fix only matters if you need a clean monotone scaling curve for the fit.

#982026-06-03T05:27:29.434Z

wouldn't increasing THINGs sampling oversample a tiny dataset? wpi;dm

You're right, that was bad advice — THINGS already gets seen ~33 times as-is. Increasing its weight just amplifies the overfitting problem for the neural heads, making things worse for the 44M model specifically.

The honest answer: this is a finding, not a bug. The 44M model exceeds the data efficiency frontier of a 30K-sample neural dataset. The scaling curve for the neural modality doesn't extend cleanly to 44M because you'd need proportionally more labeled neural data to benefit from that capacity. The 17M model is probably near the ceiling of what ~30K labeled samples can support.

For the class project this is actually a more interesting result than a clean monotone curve — it says something concrete: neural data creates a data bottleneck that breaks scaling at larger model sizes in this regime.

The only mitigation that doesn't involve more data is drop_path_rate_decoder on the 44M configs, which regularizes the neural heads without oversampling. But I'd report both the regularized and unregularized 44M results — the contrast illustrates the problem clearly.

#1012026-06-03T06:05:17.009Z

the printout reports two loss values per, one in parenthesis, what does this mean?

5 bash

The format is "{median:.4f} ({global_avg:.4f})" — the first number is the windowed median over the last 20 steps, and the number in parentheses is the global average over the entire epoch so far.

So for a modality loss like tok_meg_rvq0_loss: 3.2145 (4.8231): - 3.2145 — median of the last 20 steps (reflects current training dynamics) - (4.8231) — running mean since the epoch started (weighted by all steps, including early high-loss ones)

The gap between them tells you how fast loss is dropping: a large gap means the model is still improving rapidly within the epoch.

#1022026-06-03T07:16:22.437Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/cfgs/neural/4m/modal/model/4m-neural-3e-3d-scaling-meg-avg-rvq0.yaml in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. please explain the data bottleneck issue, how can this mechanically happen, and what evidence can i present of this?

The Mechanism

The 4M architecture has two parts: a shared backbone that processes all modalities, and modality-specific embedding/decoding heads. These train from different data pools:

  • Backbone: sees gradient from every batch — both CC12M (~270K images) and THINGS (~30K images with neural). Over 5B tokens it's very well trained.
  • Neural decoder heads: only receive gradient when a THINGS sample is in the batch (~36% of steps given your mixture weights). They learn to decode the backbone's representation into MEG codes.

The problem is that the neural decoder head is essentially learning a regression from the backbone's representation space → MEG tokens. That mapping has to be learned from ~30K labeled examples.

For the 44M model, the backbone produces 512-dim representations shaped by 300K images of pixel training. The neural head must find a reliable mapping from that 512-dim space to MEG codes using only 30K examples. For the 17M model the same problem is posed in 256-dim space — strictly easier to fit with the same sample count. More backbone capacity means a richer, more pixel-specialized representation that the neural head struggles to probe.

There's also a gradient signal ratio problem: pixel loss gets gradient from 100% of samples, neural loss from 36%. As the model grows, pixel gradients dominate the backbone update direction more strongly, pushing representations away from what the neural heads need.

Evidence You Can Present

1. The dissociation itself The cleanest evidence: plot pixel loss vs. model size and neural loss vs. model size on the same axes. Pixel loss should be monotonically decreasing; neural loss should plateau or reverse at 44M. A clean crossover is a strong, interpretable result.

2. Comparison to shuffled baseline You have shuffled ablation conditions (pixel_meg_rvq0_shuffled). The shuffled model learns the marginal token distribution — it's the floor achievable with zero image-neural alignment. If the 44M model's neural loss sits close to the shuffled baseline while the 17M model is clearly below it, that directly shows the 44M model failed to learn the image→neural mapping from 30K samples.

3. Train vs. val neural loss gap If you log training loss separately from validation: a large train/val gap on neural for the 44M model (but not 17M) is direct overfitting evidence from insufficient labeled data. The backbone generalizes fine (pixel val loss is good), only the neural head overfits.

4. Effective data per parameter framing Compute the labeled token budget for the neural modality: 30K images × 128 MEG grid cells = ~3.84M neural tokens total. Chinchilla compute-optimal training needs ~20 tokens per parameter, implying ~192K "neural-head-optimal" parameters. The 44M model's neural decoder capacity exceeds this by an order of magnitude — it has more capacity than the labeled data can regularize.

The narrative for the write-up: "The neural modality introduces a data bottleneck that breaks the scaling trend at larger model sizes. Pixel representations scale cleanly because the full 300K-image corpus provides adequate supervision at all scales. Neural representations are limited to the 10% of samples with MEG labels, creating a capacity-data mismatch that grows with model size."

#1032026-06-03T18:02:35.005Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/run_scaling_experiment_2.py in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. I find that shuffling neural recordings across the batch actually leads to better validation performance in all cases on non-neural modalities. but should I instead be shuffling neural recordings across the entire dataset rather than the batch? would this create discernable behavior effects?

Why shuffled neural helps pixel validation performance

The unshuffled neural modality places a constraint on the backbone: its representations must be simultaneously good for pixel reconstruction AND neural decoding. With only 30K labeled samples and a poorly-utilized codebook (only ~12 effective codes out of 1024), that constraint is noisy and weakly-aligned. It slightly warps the backbone away from a pixel-optimal representation.

With shuffled neural, the model learns within a few steps that neural tokens carry no image-specific information. The backbone stops being pulled toward neural-decodability and is free to optimize purely for pixels. The shuffled neural effectively acts as noise regularization — the neural decoder heads absorb gradient that would otherwise corrupt the backbone.

This is actually a meaningful result: neural data with poor alignment quality can hurt pixel representation learning. It points to the codebook collapse issue as the root cause, not just a measurement artifact.

Batch shuffle vs. dataset shuffle

The behavioral difference is real and discernible:

Batch shuffle (current): the pairing changes every step. The model can never learn a consistent image→neural mapping, even a wrong one. Neural loss stabilizes at ~2.5 because the model learns the marginal code distribution, not any image-specific pattern.

Dataset shuffle: each image is permanently paired with a specific wrong recording. Over 33 epochs, the model sees (image_A, neural_B) consistently, so it can memorize a fixed — but wrong — mapping. Neural training loss would be lower than batch shuffle (it learns the spurious pairing), but validation loss would be worse (those pairings don't generalize). This creates a train/val gap that batch shuffle doesn't produce.

For your purposes, batch shuffle is the right choice. It's a cleaner null — it answers "does image-neural alignment matter, holding neural token statistics constant?" Dataset shuffle conflates alignment with spurious memorization, making interpretation harder. The fact that batch shuffle loss stabilizes at 2.5 (not log(1024) ≈ 6.9) is your evidence of codebook underutilization, which is a separate clean finding.

The two ablations would be worth distinguishing in the write-up though: your current batch shuffle is a stronger null than dataset shuffle, which is worth noting.

This is actually a meaningful result: neural data with poor alignment quality can hurt pixel representation learning. It points to the codebook collapse issue as the root cause, not just a measurement artifact.

Batch shuffle vs. dataset shuffle

The behavioral difference is real and discernible:

Batch shuffle (current): the pairing changes every step. The model can never learn a consistent image→neural mapping, even a wrong one. Neural loss stabilizes at ~2.5 because the model learns the marginal code distribution, not any image-specific pattern.

Dataset shuffle: each image is permanently paired with a specific wrong recording. Over 33 epochs, the model sees (image_A, neural_B) consistently, so it can memorize a fixed — but wrong — mapping. Neural training loss would be lower than batch shuffle (it learns the spurious pairing), but validation loss would be worse (those pairings don't generalize). This creates a train/val gap that batch shuffle doesn't produce.

For your purposes, batch shuffle is the right choice. It's a cleaner null — it answers "does image-neural alignment matter, holding neural token statistics constant?" Dataset shuffle conflates alignment with spurious memorization, making interpretation harder. The fact that batch shuffle loss stabilizes at 2.5 (not log(1024) ≈ 6.9) is your evidence of codebook underutilization, which is a separate clean finding.

The two ablations would be worth distinguishing in the write-up though: your current batch shuffle is a stronger null than dataset shuffle, which is worth noting.

#1042026-06-03T18:07:02.905Z

explain the codebook underutilization issue, the full codebook is being utilized with high percentage across the dataset, but they might be encoding information not relevant to this task - is this what you mean?

You're right to push back — I was imprecise. Let me separate two distinct issues that I conflated.

Issue 1: Skewed marginal distribution (what actually explains the shuffled loss)

Codebook collapse (most codes never appear) is a different problem. What the shuffled loss of ~2.5 actually tells you is that the marginal distribution of codes is highly skewed — most of the probability mass sits on a small subset of codes, even if all 512 codes appear somewhere in the dataset. Coverage (which codes appear) is different from entropy (how evenly they're distributed).

Loss of 2.5 nats on a 512-vocab codebook (log(512) ≈ 6.2 nats is uniform) implies an effective entropy of e^2.5 ≈ 12 codes worth of information. That's consistent with, say, 15 codes accounting for 70% of assignments and the remaining 497 codes sharing the other 30%. Every code appears, high coverage, but the distribution is highly peaked. The shuffled model just learns to predict that peaked marginal and gets low loss without seeing a single image.

Issue 2: Low image-code mutual information (your correct intuition)

This is the deeper problem. Even if the codebook had uniform marginal distribution (high entropy), the model can only beat the shuffled baseline by exploiting actual mutual information between image content and MEG codes. MEG contains a lot of signal that isn't image-specific:

  • Baseline neural activity, arousal, attention state
  • Trial-to-trial variability in response magnitude
  • Neural dynamics that the VQ tokenizer captures but that don't reliably vary with image content

So the gap between the shuffled baseline (~2.5) and the real model is your empirical estimate of how much image-neural mutual information the tokenizer actually captures. A small gap means the codes are encoding mostly noise from the perspective of image prediction.

Why tok_meg_avg is the direct fix

Trial averaging is specifically designed to address issue 2: it cancels the trial-to-trial noise that's uncorrelated with the stimulus, leaving only the stimulus-locked component. If tok_meg_avg narrows the gap to the shuffled baseline more than tok_meg does, that's direct evidence that trial noise was the main barrier — not the tokenizer architecture.

#1052026-06-03T18:40:18.960Z

the averaging only seems to marginally improve the 27 way categorization above random - is it worth running experiments on these?

Probably not worth it. Here's the reasoning:

The 27-way categorization result is your empirical bound on the mutual information the averaged MEG tokens carry about image content. If that's barely above chance at the representational level, the 4M model has almost nothing to learn from the image→neural direction. The loss curves will be noisy, the shuffled baseline gap will be small, and Chinchilla fitting on those curves won't give clean scaling coefficients.

The experiments would consume ~4 additional conditions worth of GPU budget to likely reproduce the same data bottleneck finding with slightly different numbers — not a qualitatively different result.

The more interesting scientific question your data is already set up to answer is:

  • Does adding weak neural signal hurt or help pixel scaling? (You already have this with tok_meg)
  • What is the shuffled baseline gap? (Quantifies image-neural MI for the tokenizer)
  • Where does the data bottleneck break the scaling curve? (The 44M finding)

Those three findings together tell a coherent story about the limits of neural tokenization for cross-modal learning, and tok_meg_avg would be a footnote rather than a chapter.

If you do want a one-sentence mention of it in the write-up: "trial averaging of MEG responses improved 27-way categorization only marginally above chance, suggesting the VQ tokenizer does not preserve sufficient stimulus-locked information for reliable cross-modal alignment regardless of trial noise."

#1062026-06-03T18:42:36.179Z

is it worth trying to run some experiments on potentially better regularizers, like text?

Not worth it for this project. The reasoning:

The bottleneck is on the MEG representation side, not the image representation side. Text supervision would give the backbone richer semantic representations, but the neural decoder heads still have to decode those into MEG tokens that barely encode category information (as shown by the 27-way result). Better backbone representations don't help if the tokenizer is the ceiling.

Text would improve pixel scaling (well-established), might marginally improve neural performance, but wouldn't change the fundamental story — it would just shift all curves down slightly without altering their relative behavior or the data bottleneck finding.

There are also two practical problems:

  1. Budget: A full text scaling sweep means rerunning all conditions with a new modality configuration — roughly doubling the experiment cost at a point where you already have a complete story.

  2. Interpretability: If text helps neural performance, you can't cleanly attribute it. Is it because better semantic representations align with neural data? Or because text and neural both encode semantic categories and the model finds a shortcut? It muddies the conclusions rather than sharpening them.

The findings you have are already a complete, coherent story: scaling behavior with and without neural data, data bottleneck at 44M, shuffled baseline quantifying image-neural MI, and the codebook entropy issue. Text would be a new research question, not a clarification of the current one.

If you wanted to strengthen the story with one more data point, the higher-leverage thing would be a brief linear probe analysis on the backbone representations — does adding (unshuffled) neural data change what the backbone learns about image content, and does this vary by model size? That directly tests whether neural data affects the shared representations without requiring new training runs.

#1072026-06-03T18:43:35.337Z

what I mean is replacing neural modality with text

That's a much better idea — it's a positive control, not a distraction. Here's why it's worth it:

The core scientific question is whether the poor neural scaling is due to: - (A) Low image-neural mutual information (the tokenizer / neural signal quality), or - (B) Something about how 4M handles auxiliary modalities at scale

You can't distinguish A from B with neural data alone. A text condition directly answers this: text captions have very high MI with image content, CC12M already has them, and the 4M infrastructure supports text natively. If the text condition shows clean monotonic scaling, it confirms your 4M setup works correctly and the neural findings are specifically due to low MI — a much stronger claim than you can make right now.

The comparison becomes: "well-aligned auxiliary modality (text) scales cleanly; poorly-aligned auxiliary modality (neural) does not." That's a clean result.

Practical considerations:

  • CC12M has captions; THINGS likely doesn't, but that's fine — text would be a CC12M-only modality, the inverse asymmetry of neural (THINGS-only). Worth noting as a difference.
  • You'd need one new alpha config (rgb-depth-text-bidirectional), one data config, and 2 model configs (2e2d + 3e3d). The text modality is already registered in 4M's default modality_info.
  • One sweep (4 model sizes, 1 condition) to establish the text scaling curve. No shuffled variants needed — text isn't your focus.

Budget-wise it's one new arm, which is probably your highest-value remaining experiment given what it would let you conclude about the neural findings.

#1082026-06-03T18:45:59.766Z

to follow the same methodology, I would probably want to only use text on the things images.. any suggestions on how to proceed? should I use a captioning model on the 25,000 images?

3 bash · 1 read

For a positive control you don't need rich captions — you need high MI with image content. THINGS already gives you that for free: every image has a category label (1854 categories). Use the template "a photo of [category_name]" and tokenize offline. That's it.

This is actually a cleaner positive control than generated captions because: - MI is maximally high (label is exact ground truth) - No captioning model noise or hallucinations - Reproducible and free - The simplicity isolates exactly what you're testing — MI quality vs. neural's low MI

Practical path:

  1. Extract THINGS category labels (already in the THINGS dataset metadata)
  2. Format as "a photo of {name}", tokenize with 4M's text tokenizer offline
  3. Pack into WDS shards alongside the existing THINGS modalities (same shard structure as tok_meg etc.)
  4. New alpha config + data config, same model configs as neural arm

One infrastructure question first: check what text modality 4M's modality_info.py has registered and whether it expects pretokenized T5 embeddings, raw BPE tokens, or something else. That determines the tokenization step. What does grep -i "caption\|text\|t5" /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/fourm/data/modality_info.py | head -20 show?

Good news: CaptionTransform reads raw .txt or .json files — no pretokenization or T5 inference needed. The tokenization happens downstream in the 4M pipeline. So the on-disk format is just plain text strings.

The full path:

  1. Generate the text: THINGS has structured category metadata. "a photo of {category_name}" per image, written as one .txt file per sample. This is a 10-line Python script against the THINGS metadata — no captioning model needed.

  2. Pack into WDS shards: Create caption-only shards at /project/data/[train,val]/things/caption/shard_{000..026}.tar, matching the same shard structure as tok_meg, meg_mask etc. Each sample gets a {image_id}.caption.txt entry.

  3. New configs: One alpha config (rgb-caption-bidirectional-all2all.yaml), one data config pointing at [tok_rgb,tok_depth,caption]/shard_*.tar, two model configs. The caption modality is already registered in 4M — no Python changes needed.

  4. Alpha config note: caption is type: seq with max_tokens: 256. For "a photo of [category]" you'll only use 5–8 tokens, so set max_tokens low (e.g. 16) in the alpha config to avoid allocating empty token budget.

Before committing to this, worth checking: does the THINGS dataset file you have include category name metadata, or is it purely image files? If category names aren't readily accessible, that changes the packing step slightly.

#1092026-06-03T20:33:38.586Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/run_scaling_experiment_2.py in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. Can you set up an inference run that evaluates a selected model's RGB -> Depth performance on an image in THINGS, and then reconstruct an image from the tokens? I'm using 4M's default depth tokenizer

9 read · 4 bash · 2 agent · 1 edit · 1 toolsearch · 1 exitplanmode · 1 write

Done. The script is at ml-4m/run_things_inference.py.

Three things to note when you run it:

  1. Checkpoint path — points to the .pth file inside your Modal output volume, e.g. …/checkpoints/checkpoint-latest.pth or checkpoint-epoch5.pth

  2. Data config path resolution — the model YAMLs have /opt/repo/ml-4m/cfgs/… hardcoded. The script tries a local fallback if that path doesn't exist, mapping it relative to the repo root. If that also fails it'll say so clearly.

  3. Tokenizer download — DiVAE.from_pretrained(…) hits HuggingFace on first run and caches locally. On Modal it'll re-download each invocation unless you cache it in the image or volume. A quick fix when running frequently: pre-download once and pass local paths instead of Hub IDs.

#1102026-06-03T21:02:45.999Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/README_GENERATION.md in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. I'd like the option to use a fixed sample from a shard so I can compare generation outputs across models, please check that is possible. can I get it to download once on modal and cache it seomwhere?

3 edit · 1 bash

Both features are wired in. How to use them:

Fixed keys across models — first run without --sample_key and note the key labels in the output (e.g. 000042), then pin them for all subsequent model comparisons:

python run_things_inference.py \
  --checkpoint /project/output/pixel_only/2layer/dim128/checkpoints/checkpoint-latest.pth \
  --config ... --things_root /project/data/val/things \
  --sample_key 000042,000117,000203,000389 \
  --output /project/output/inference/pixel_only_2l_128.png

Persistent tokenizer cache on Modal — add --tokenizer_cache /project/hf_cache to any invocation. First run downloads ~1-2 GB to the project volume; every run after reads from there instantly. The env var HF_HOME is set before the from_pretrained calls so it takes effect automatically.

#1112026-06-03T21:08:35.753Z

can the model generate RGB + MEG -> depth even though this wasn't an explicit task learned?

Yes — and it was actually trained on this exact combination, just not as a dedicated task.

The all2all mixture component (the third component in rgb-meg-bidirectional-all2all.yaml, weighted 33%) assigns each modality a Dirichlet(0.5) input budget independently. That means combinations like (RGB + MEG → depth) occur during training with nonzero probability. The model never saw it as an exclusive task, but it's within the training distribution.

The 4M architecture handles it naturally: the encoder attends over all visible tokens regardless of modality type, so it can jointly encode RGB tokens (196 positions) and MEG grid tokens (128 positions) and the decoder generates depth conditioned on that combined representation.

Whether it's better than RGB alone is the empirical question — and given the low MEG-image MI you've already observed, the answer is probably "not much." But it's a direct way to test whether the model learned to leverage MEG for depth prediction at all. If the depth prediction improves with MEG added as input, that's evidence the model found a useful cross-modal signal despite the noisy tokenization.

To try it in the inference script, you'd need to: 1. Load tok_meg (or tok_meg_avg) tokens from the shard — shape (1, 16, 8, 4) → apply MegTrialSampleTransform → (128,) per RVQ layer 2. Add each tok_meg_rvq{q} to mod_dict as a full input (the defaults in init_full_input_modality work correctly for neural_grid type — it falls through to input_mask=all False, target_mask=all True which is exactly "full input") 3. Add all four to cond_domains in the generation schedule

The generate() dispatch only acts on the target modality (tok_depth@224), so the MEG tokens just sit in mod_dict as encoder context without needing any special handling.

Want me to add a --include_meg flag to the inference script to enable this?

# First find some fixed keys with the pixel-only model

# Then use the same keys with a MEG-trained model, with and without MEG input
python run_things_inference.py \

3 bash · 2 write · 1 read

Done. All models run in a single T4 container — tokenizers and THINGS samples load once, then each model checkpoint is loaded, run, and freed before the next.

T4 is the right GPU here. Total weight budget at any point: ~500 MB FM model + ~350 MB RGB tokenizer + ~350 MB depth tokenizer = ~1.2 GB. A T4's 16 GB gives you ~13× headroom even with activations for batch-size-1 inference.

Key design choice — models run sequentially rather than in parallel containers: startup + HF download (~90 seconds) is paid once instead of N times. For 4 models that's a ~6 minute saving.

The --include_meg_for flag lets you mix RGB-only and RGB+MEG runs for the same checkpoint without needing separate entries:

modal run 4m_training/modal_inference.py \
  --names meg_rgb,meg_rgb_plus_meg \
  --checkpoints /project/.../checkpoint-latest.pth,/project/.../checkpoint-latest.pth \
  --configs /opt/repo/.../4m-neural-3e-3d-scaling-meg-rvq0.yaml,/opt/repo/.../4m-neural-3e-3d-scaling-meg-rvq0.yaml \
  --sample_key 000042,000117,000203,000389 \
  --include_meg_for meg_rgb_plus_meg

The problem is clear: inference_job does sys.path.insert to find fourm source files but never runs ensure_fourm(), so fourm's pip deps (albumentations, diffusers, huggingface_hub, etc.) are never installed. The training script calls ensure_fourm() first thing, which does the editable install and pulls all deps. The inference script skips that step.

The minimal fix is not to enumerate all missing packages — it's to call ensure_fourm() the same way training does.

You can also revert the boto3 addition to modal_image.py if you want — ensure_fourm() will install it as a transitive dep anyway. But keeping it there is harmless (it just deduplicates a package that's already installed), so leaving it is fine.

The root cause: sys.path.insert makes Python find fourm source files, but fourm's __init__.py chains import through albumentations, diffusers, huggingface_hub, etc., which are not in the base image. ensure_fourm() runs pip install -e ml-4m which installs all 20+ deps from pyproject.toml before any of those imports fire.

#1232026-06-03T22:44:00.413Z

1 bash · 1 edit

weights_only=False disables PyTorch 2.4+'s default restriction that only allows tensors/dicts/lists in checkpoints. The training script saves the full checkpoint dict (model state + optimizer state + epoch counters etc.), which contains Python objects that the strict loader rejects. This is safe here since these are your own checkpoints.

#1242026-06-03T22:53:47.045Z

how long should I expect inference to take per model?

For a 44M parameter model on a T4:

  • Model load + checkpoint: ~5-10s
  • Tokenizer loads (both DiVAE, first run downloading from HF): ~60-90s first time, ~10s from cache
  • 4 samples × RGB→Depth generation: ROAR with 1 decoding step is essentially a single forward pass per sample → ~1-2s total for all 4 samples
  • Decoding tokens back to images: ~2-4s per tokenizer call × 3 calls (RGB GT, depth GT, depth pred) × 4 samples → ~5-10s

Total per model: ~30-60s (after first HF download). With 2 models in one run, expect ~90s after tokenizers are loaded.

On CPU: the forward pass for a 44M transformer with batch size 1 is ~30-60s per sample, so 4 samples → ~3-5 minutes per model. The smaller 7M models would be ~30-60s per model on CPU. CPU is fine for debugging with 1 sample (--n_samples 1) but slow for a full comparison grid.

The dominant cost at small batch sizes is actually the DiVAE decode calls (CNN upsampling to 224×224), not the FM transformer — so model size matters less than you'd expect.

#1252026-06-03T22:56:13.517Z
