The GPU spike is from tokenizer loading, and then the process could be stuck anywhere after — THINGS shard reading, model load, or generation. The most useful thing is verbose prints at every step. Also, matplotlib.use('Agg') is missing — on a headless container it can hang if it tries to initialize a GUI backend, particularly after the GPU has already warmed up.

Two likely culprits: 1. matplotlib — without use('Agg') it tries to initialize a GUI backend on the headless container, which hangs silently after GPU setup 2. Print buffering — Modal may not flush stdout without PYTHONUNBUFFERED, so you see silence even when work is running

Let me fix both and add step-level prints so you can see exactly where it stalls:

Key changes: 1. matplotlib.use("Agg") before any pyplot import — prevents the GUI backend initialization hang on headless containers. This is the most likely root cause. 2. sys.stdout.reconfigure(line_buffering=True) — forces every print to flush immediately instead of batching; Modal log streaming requires this. 3. flush=True on every print — belt-and-suspenders alongside reconfigure. 4. verbose=True in sampler.generate() — enables tqdm so you can see if the generation loop is running or stuck. 5. Step-level prints — you'll now see exactly which line the output stops at: tokenizer load → THINGS load → model build → sample N → generate → decode → save.

#1262026-06-03T23:03:29.201Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/4m_training/modal_inference.py in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. please explain how the pipeline works. the model generates prediction tokens, which then goes through some diffusion model, or what happens?

No diffusion — it's fully discrete token prediction throughout. Here's the full pipeline:

Stage 0 — Pre-tokenization (already done, stored on disk)

Both RGB images and depth maps were passed through VQ-VAE encoders offline. Each 224×224 image becomes a flat sequence of 196 integer codes (14×14 grid, codebook size 16k for RGB, 8k for depth). These are stored as .npy int16 arrays in the THINGS shard tars. The FM never sees raw pixels — only token indices.

Stage 1 — The FM generates depth token predictions

The 4M foundation model is a masked transformer (encoder-decoder). At inference:

  1. RGB tokens (196 integers) → encoder embeddings → transformer encoder → context
  2. Depth token positions initialized as masked/empty → decoder attends to encoder context
  3. ROAR (Random Order AutoRegression) with decoding_steps=1 predicts all 196 depth tokens in one parallel shot — this is one forward pass (plus a second for CFG, explained below)

Each output token is a logit distribution over the depth codebook (8k entries). Temperature 0.01 makes this near-deterministic (essentially argmax). CFG scale 2.0 runs the forward pass twice — once with RGB conditioning, once without (empty RGB) — and sharpens the conditioning: logits = logits_uncond + 2.0 * (logits_cond - logits_uncond).

Output: 196 depth token indices (integers from 0–8191).

Stage 2 — DiVAE decodes tokens back to an image

The predicted 196 integers are passed to DiVAE.decode_tokens(tokens_2d):

  1. Codebook lookup: each integer → its learned embedding vector
  2. Reshape to (14, 14, D) spatial feature map
  3. CNN decoder (convolutional upsampling) → 224×224 float map

This is a learned CNN decoder, not diffusion. The VQ-VAE codebook was trained to compress depth maps into discrete codes and reconstruct them faithfully — it's the same decoder that was used to tokenize the training data, so the round-trip (image → codes → image) is lossless within the VQ-VAE's capacity.

What you're measuring

The quality gap between GT depth (re-decoded from ground-truth tokens) and predicted depth (decoded from FM-generated tokens) is purely the FM's prediction quality. The DiVAE decoder is the same in both cases, so it cancels out.

#1272026-06-03T23:07:12.406Z

decoding tokens is where it spends the most time. is that loaded onto the GPU?

Yes, the tokenizers are explicitly moved to the device in _run_inference:

tok_rgb_dec = DiVAE.from_pretrained(...).to(device).eval()
tok_depth_dec = DiVAE.from_pretrained(...).to(device).eval()

But decode_to_image immediately moves the result back to CPU:

img = tokenizer.decode_tokens(tokens_2d)   # runs on GPU
img = img.squeeze(0).cpu().float()         # .cpu() — synchronizes and transfers

The .cpu() call forces a CUDA synchronization point — if the GPU decode is async-queued, .cpu() blocks until it completes before transferring. For 3 decode calls × 4 samples = 12 round-trips, that synchronization overhead adds up.

The DiVAE decoder is a convolutional upsampler (14×14 → 224×224). On a T4 it should be fast (~10-50ms per call), so 12 calls should be under a second. If it's taking longer, the more likely culprit is the FM forward passes — with CFG, that's 2 forward passes × 4 samples = 8 passes through a 44M transformer. Each pass has ~400 tokens (196 RGB + 196 depth + registers). On a T4 that's probably 200-500ms each → 1.5-4 seconds total for generation, not the decode.

To confirm which is slow, the step-level prints added in the last edit will tell you — you'll see the timestamp gap between "Generating…" and "Decoding tokens…" vs "Decoding tokens…" and "Row N done.".

No — drop_path_rate_encoder/drop_path_rate_decoder are parameters of fm.py:101-102, so the model supports them, but run_training_4m.py:379-385 calls create_model without passing them:

model = create_model(
    args.model,
    encoder_embeddings=encoder_embeddings,
    decoder_embeddings=decoder_embeddings,
    modality_info=modality_info,
    num_register_tokens=args.num_register_tokens,
    # drop_path_rate_encoder never passed → always 0.0
)

There's also no --drop_path_rate argparse argument in the trainer, so the YAML set_defaults path doesn't work either.

To actually use it you'd need to wire it up in lib/train_4m.py. The cleanest place is inside _drive_trainer_main (or wherever you hook into the model creation) — read drop_path_rate off trainer.args and patch the model after it's constructed, or patch run_training_4m.py's create_model call via the same monkey-patch mechanism you use for token accounting.

The practical alternative that requires no code changes: weight decay (weight_decay: 0.1 in the model YAML) is properly wired through argparse at run_training_4m.py:108 and does work directly from the config. That's the regularization knob you can actually turn today without touching Python.

#1542026-06-03T23:09:24.232Z

it's hitting "Decoding tokens..." and then hanging there, why might these be?

13 bash · 5 read · 1 edit

Found the root cause. DiVAE uses a diffusion decoder — decode_tokens without a timesteps argument runs all 1000 training timesteps instead of the 25 the demo uses. At 1000 DDIM steps × 3 decode calls × 4 samples = 12,000 forward passes, that's 10+ minutes just for decoding.

The fix: pass timesteps=25 to decode_to_image.

Both tokenizers are DiVAE (diffusion decoders, not CNNs). Without timesteps=, the pipeline defaults to num_train_timesteps=1000 — which at even 5ms/step × 1000 steps × 3 decodes × 4 samples = 60 seconds minimum, often much more. With timesteps=25 (what the 4M demo uses), decode time drops to ~1.5 seconds total.

#1552026-06-03T23:21:16.701Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/ml-4m/run_things_inference.py in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. how is depth gt generated? it's passed through the tokenizer?

1 bash

Yes — the depth GT shown in the figure is the pre-tokenized depth codes loaded from disk (tok_depth/shard_NNN.tar), then decoded back through the same DiVAE tokenizer.

The pipeline is:

Original depth map → DiVAE encoder → 196 int codes → stored in shard
                                                           ↓  (on disk)
                                                      tok_depth_t
                                                           ↓
                                          DiVAE decoder (25 DDIM steps)
                                                           ↓
                                                     "GT depth" image

So the GT isn't the original depth image — it's the tokenizer's reconstruction of it. This is intentional: it makes the GT and predicted depth directly comparable on the same tokenizer's output manifold (any quality floor from tokenization is equal for both). The model predicts token indices, so the only meaningful comparison is between two decoded reconstructions from the same codebook.

#1562026-06-03T23:22:02.234Z

the generated GT looks like a blobby mess.. should I be concerned

1 bash · 1 read · 1 edit

Two issues in decode_to_image:

  1. Wrong normalization: The raw decoded depth values are not in [0, 1] — they're in some arbitrary float range from the DiVAE decoder. Clipping to [0, 1] produces a saturated black/white blob. The 4M demo does min-max normalization ((img - min) / (max - min)) to stretch the full depth range to [0, 1].

  2. Missing image_size=224: Without it the pipeline uses its own default which may differ.

Without min-max normalization, if the decoder outputs values in e.g. [−2, 8], clipping to [0, 1] turns everything below 0 black and everything above 1 white — leaving only a thin slice of the actual depth gradient visible, which looks like a blob. The min-max stretch fixes that. The same image_size=224 fix also applies to the RGB decode (no functional change there since RGB values already land near [0, 1] after the * 0.5 + 0.5).

#1572026-06-03T23:31:48.591Z

do you think I should try to fine tune the original small model with neural data? how long would that take? I'd only have one gpu without any significant changes

It's an interesting idea but it depends on what question you're trying to answer.

The case for it: The pre-trained 4M-21 model has a strong visual backbone already — adding neural modality heads on top by fine-tuning only the new encoder/decoder embeddings while freezing the rest would be fast. With only 26k THINGS samples, training the neural heads to convergence is maybe 10–20 epochs × 1600 steps = 20–30k steps. On a single A100 that's ~20 minutes. On a consumer GPU (3090/4090) more like 45–90 minutes.

The catch: You can't just load the pre-trained model and call .to(device) — the pre-trained 4M-21 has no tok_meg_rvq* or tok_eeg heads. You'd need to: 1. Instantiate the model with the pre-trained config + your new modality entries 2. Load the pre-trained weights for all existing heads 3. Initialize the new neural heads randomly 4. Fine-tune with the neural heads unfrozen (and optionally the last N decoder layers)

That's a moderate amount of glue code. Whether it's worth it depends on your goal:

  • If your goal is the scaling law experiment — don't bother. That experiment needs from-scratch training at multiple model sizes, and the pre-trained model is one point, not a curve.
  • If your goal is "can neural data reconstruct visual stimuli" — yes, this is probably the fastest path to a compelling qualitative result. A fine-tuned 4M with MEG→RGB inference would be a stronger demo than your small from-scratch models.

If you have a class deadline and the scaling runs are still going, fine-tuning the pre-trained small model for a qualitative figure could be a good parallel bet.

#1582026-06-03T23:40:54.701Z

you mention fine tuning the neural encoder/decoders embeddings, but maybe we can fine tune the rgb and depth heads as well?

Yes, and it's actually the better approach for your goal. A few considerations:

Why fine-tuning RGB/depth heads makes sense here: You're not trying to preserve general visual capabilities — you only care about THINGS images. Fine-tuning all heads on THINGS means the RGB and depth representations shift toward that domain (clean object photos, specific depth profiles), which should improve neural↔visual alignment specifically for your eval set. Catastrophic forgetting of broader visual knowledge doesn't matter if you only evaluate on THINGS.

The real constraint is memory: Fine-tuning all heads requires gradients + Adam states for the entire model, roughly 4× model size in VRAM. The pre-trained 4M variants are large (the 4M-21 model is ~700M params → ~11GB for weights+optimizer). On a single consumer GPU (16GB) that's borderline. Options if you hit the limit:

  • Gradient checkpointing — trades compute for memory, ~20% slower
  • Frozen transformer backbone, fine-tune only embeddings — the middle ground: freeze the encoder/decoder transformer weights, only train the modality-specific embedding projections (neural + RGB + depth). This cuts gradient memory dramatically since the big matmuls don't need to retain activations.
  • LoRA on the transformer layers — low-rank updates only, very memory efficient, widely used for this kind of domain adaptation

Recommended approach for a single GPU: Freeze the core transformer weights, fine-tune all modality embedding heads (neural + RGB + depth). This gives you domain adaptation for THINGS visual representations without the memory explosion, and the neural heads still learn to align with the adapted visual space. Training time stays in the 30–90 minute range.

The harder part remains the glue code to load pre-trained weights + inject new modality heads — that's probably 1–2 hours of implementation work.

#1612026-06-04T03:03:22.679Z

Instead of this, I would like to use DINO embeddings in place of neural data. this means I would need to run inference with DINO on my images, and use 4M's pretrained tokenizer to tokenize the output. I will likely do this on modal, please come up with an implementation plan

7 bash · 7 write · 5 edit · 4 read · 3 exitplanmode · 2 agent

DINOv2 is the better choice for replacing neural data. Here's why:

DINOv2 (tok_dinov2@224): - Self-supervised, trained to produce spatially consistent dense features — no language prior - 256 tokens at 14px patch size → finer spatial resolution than CLIP - Multiple neuroscience studies show DINOv2 better predicts fMRI/EEG/MEG responses in visual cortex (V1–IT) than CLIP, because visual areas encode spatial structure, not semantic categories - 4M-21_B's dinov2 head is pre-trained; simple VQVAE decoder (fast, no diffusion)

CLIP (tok_clip@224): - Contrastively trained on image-text pairs → encodes semantic category more than spatial structure - 196 tokens at 16px patches → coarser spatial resolution - Better proxy for what is in the image (semantic), not how it activates visual cortex - Would be more useful if you were trying to align with higher-level areas (anterior IT, PFC) or language-based neural responses

For MEG/EEG as the target, which primarily reflects spatiotemporal visual processing across early-to-mid visual areas, DINOv2 is the more scientifically grounded choice.

That said, since both tokenizers are available in 4M-21_B and the implementation is near-identical, the plan can trivially support both — separate shard folders + configs. I'll update the plan to produce tok_dinov2@224 and tok_clip@224 shards in Phase 1 and add a separate CLIP fine-tune config, so you can run both and compare directly.

All done. Here's a summary of what was created/changed:

7 bash · 4 read

Confirmed: crop_settings is NOT needed. Here's exactly why:

lib/train_4m.py calls patch_pretrain_utils() at import time (fourm_dataloader.py:318-319), which replaces PreTokenizedImageAugmenter with ThingsImageAugmenter. That class explicitly handles the missing crop_settings case (things_augmenter.py:57-59):

def __call__(self, mod_dict, crop_settings):
    if crop_settings is None:
        crop_settings = DEFAULT_CROP_SETTINGS  # fixed center-crop fallback

The stock PreTokenizedImageAugmenter (which crashes on None) is never used when going through lib/train_4m.py.

One shape detail to note:

fourm_neural_modalities.py only patches ThingsTokTransform (which handles flat 1D arrays) for tok_rgb and tok_depth (fourm_neural_modalities.py:172-173). tok_dinov2@224 keeps the stock TokTransform, which does v[rand_aug_idx] — so it expects a 2D array and indexes into it:

  • (1, 256)[0] → (256,) ✓ our current shape
  • (256,)[0] → a single scalar ✗ would silently corrupt the input

Our tokenizer writing (1, 256) int16 is exactly right. The format is fully compatible.

Good questions. Let me work through each:

1. GPU — You don't. The script defaults to cpu. No GPU needed.

2. Token format per sample — Each shard entry is (1, 256) int16: the 1 is the crop axis (single center crop), 256 is the flat 16×16 spatial grid of token IDs from the 8192-vocab codebook.

3. VQVAE — You don't need it. I added it to get a "meaningful" raw bracket by decoding tokens back to features, but that's the wrong framing for an eval that starts from tokens. The codebook and sequence axes need only token IDs. The probe and retrieval axes can use bag-of-codes (a histogram over the 8192-vocab — which codes appear, how often) as features, which requires zero additional models. The signal argument those axes require becomes a dummy (N, 1, 1) tensor; the raw bracket from it is at chance, which is actually the correct lower bound since we have no raw features.

Let me rewrite to remove the VQVAE entirely:

The rewrite removes the VQVAE entirely. The script is now: - No GPU required — pure CPU, no model loading - No downloads — works from pre-computed token IDs only - Token format: each sample is (1, 256) int16 on disk — 256 flat token IDs from the 16×16 spatial grid, with the leading 1 being the single center-crop axis - Features: bag-of-codes (normalised histogram over the 8192-code vocab) — sufficient for probe and retrieval without any model - raw bracket: dummy (N, 1, 1) signal → lands at chance (3.7%), which is correct since we have no raw visual features to compare against

#1772026-06-04T22:28:57.453Z

The user opened the file /home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/neural_tokenizers/evaluation/eval_dinov2.py in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task. does the modal volume put code work across workspaces?

No — modal volume put/get only works within your currently authenticated workspace. There's no direct cross-workspace transfer command.

The workflow to copy between accounts:

# 1. While authenticated as the shared account — download to local
modal volume get project data/val/things/tok_dinov2@224 ./tok_dinov2@224_local

# 2. Switch to your own account (re-auth or switch profile)
modal token set --token-id <your-id> --token-secret <your-secret>

# 3. Upload to your volume
modal volume put project ./tok_dinov2@224_local data/val/things/tok_dinov2@224

The data transfer through local is fine here — the token shards are small. Each sample is 256 tokens × 2 bytes (int16) = 512 bytes, so 26k THINGS images ≈ ~13 MB per split. Fast to download and re-upload.

If you have multiple Modal profiles configured you can use modal --profile <name> to switch without re-authing each time.

#1782026-06-04T22:29:58.498Z

I would need both train and val right? i'd like to run the evaluation on the whole dataset

For the evaluation as currently written, you only need val — the probe uses k-fold CV internally so it doesn't need a separate train split. Val alone gives you a clean held-out evaluation.

That said, if you want the whole dataset (more statistical power for the probe/retrieval metrics), you'd need both — but the current load_tokens_from_shards takes a single directory. The train and val shards likely have overlapping key names (shard_000.tar exists in both), so you'd need to either:

Option A (simplest): Load both dirs and prefix keys to avoid collisions:

train_dict = {f"train/{k}": v for k, v in load_tokens_from_shards(train_dir).items()}
val_dict   = {f"val/{k}": v   for k, v in load_tokens_from_shards(val_dir).items()}
all_tokens = {**train_dict, **val_dict}

But this breaks the label lookup since load_things_labels strips leading zeros from the key to get image_id — prefixing would break that.

Option B (cleaner): Check if THINGS keys are actually globally unique across splits (likely yes, since they map to specific image IDs). If so, just load both dirs directly and merge — duplicate keys would just overwrite harmlessly.

For a quick check: do the train and val shards use non-overlapping image IDs, or do they reuse key names like 000001?

If the image IDs don't overlap across splits, you can just load both directories sequentially into the same dict and everything works. If they do overlap, option A needs a fix to the label chain.

Either way you'd only need to copy both splits (~13 MB each) to your volume.

You need three things: download the shards, install dependencies, and make a small change to support loading from two directories (since train and val have same shard filenames but non-overlapping keys).

Step 1 — Download from Modal volume:

modal volume get project data/val/things/tok_dinov2@224 ./data/val/tok_dinov2@224
modal volume get project data/train/things/tok_dinov2@224 ./data/train/tok_dinov2@224

Step 2 — Install dependencies:

pip install torch numpy scikit-learn

Step 3 — Small script change to accept multiple token dirs:

Now the run command for the full dataset:

# Download from Modal
modal volume get project data/train/things/tok_dinov2@224 ./tok_dinov2_train
modal volume get project data/val/things/tok_dinov2@224   ./tok_dinov2_val

# Run eval on both splits combined
python neural_tokenizers/evaluation/eval_dinov2.py \
    --tokens_dir ./tok_dinov2_train ./tok_dinov2_val

The label files are already present at their default paths relative to the repo, so no extra args needed for those. --shard_indices still works if you want to subset (e.g. --shard_indices 0 1 2 applies to each directory).

The other axis modules (codebook.py, etc.) use relative imports internally, so they must be imported as part of the package — not as standalone files. The fix is to run as a module from the repo root:

cd /path/to/neural-foundation-model
python -m neural_tokenizers.evaluation.eval_dinov2 \
    --tokens_dir ./tok_dinov2_train ./tok_dinov2_val

With -m, Python imports eval_dinov2 in the neural_tokenizers.evaluation package context, so both the from .protocol import ... in eval_dinov2.py and the from .protocol import ... inside codebook.py etc. resolve correctly. The fallback sys.path branch never fires.

Loading THINGS labels … Traceback (most recent call last): File "/home/users/liubr/micromamba/envs/fourm/lib/python3.9/runpy.py", line 197, in _run_module_as_main return _run_code(code, main_globals, None, File "/home/users/liubr/micromamba/envs/fourm/lib/python3.9/runpy.py", line 87, in _run_code exec(code, run_globals) File "/home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/neural_tokenizers/evaluation/eval_dinov2.py", line 357, in main() File "/home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/neural_tokenizers/evaluation/eval_dinov2.py", line 339, in main run_eval( File "/home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/neural_tokenizers/evaluation/eval_dinov2.py", line 229, in run_eval labels, category_names = load_things_labels( File "/home/users/liubr/projects/neural-image-foundation/neural-foundation-model/neural_tokenizers/evaluation/eval_dinov2.py", line 175, in load_things_labels raise RuntimeError( RuntimeError: Could not resolve labels for 15300 keys (first few: ['000000013', '000000014', '000000015', '000000016', '000000017']). Check that --tokens_dir and --image_id_to_concept are aligned.

The image_id_to_concept.json only covers ~22,448 images with MEG trials, but the DINOv2 shards cover all 26,107 THINGS images. The missing 15,300 are THINGS images that weren't used in MEG — there's nothing wrong with the key format, just a coverage gap.

The fix is to skip unlabeled images rather than hard-failing, and filter the token dict to only labeled samples:

Found it — concept_id_to_superordinate_index only covers 892 out of ~1854 concepts. Concept 2 (which covers images like '15') has no superordinate label. That's the bottleneck: images whose concept lacks a superordinate entry are silently dropped by the chain.

So the ~10,800 that DO resolve = images in MEG + whose concept is in the 892-concept superordinate map. The user's concern is valid — it's not MEG coverage that's limiting, it's the 27-way superordinate labels only covering half the THINGS concepts.

The options: 1. Keep current approach (27-way superordinate, ~10k samples) — skip unlabeled, warn about it 2. Probe at concept level instead — use image_id_to_concept_id directly for a 1854-way probe, covers all 22,448 MEG images

For a fair comparison with MEG/EEG evals (which use the same superordinate labels), option 1 is probably right. Let me apply the skip-with-warning fix:

1 bash · 1 write

Created at average_depth_maps.py. Usage:

# Basic — scans one flat directory
python average_depth_maps.py --depth_dir /path/to/depth_maps

# Recurse into subdirectories (e.g. organised by category or split)
python average_depth_maps.py --depth_dir /path/to/depth_maps --recursive

# Also save the float32 average array
python average_depth_maps.py --depth_dir /path/to/depth_maps --save_npy

Only needs numpy and matplotlib. Handles a few edge cases: arrays with a leading batch/channel dim (squeezed), mismatched spatial sizes (bilinearly resized to match the first image — needs Pillow), and skips files with unexpected shapes with a warning.
